<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">New Testament</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Christian Greek Scriptures that are contained in the <a href="/wiki/Christian_biblical_canons" title="Christian biblical canons">Christian Biblical canon</a>. For the theological concept, see <a href="/wiki/New_Covenant" title="New Covenant">New Covenant</a>.</div>

</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>

<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" title="Christian cross"><img alt="Christian cross" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="400" height="18" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/20px-P_christianity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/30px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/40px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></td>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template talk:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Christianity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>New Testament</b> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Ἡ Καινὴ Διαθήκη</span>, <i>Hē Kainḕ Diathḗkē</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la"><i>Novum Testamentum</i></span>) is the second part of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_biblical_canons" title="Christian biblical canons">Christian biblical canon</a>, the first part being the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, based on the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>. The New Testament discusses the teachings and person of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus</a>, as well as events in <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_1st_century" title="Christianity in the 1st century">first-century Christianity</a>. Christians regard both the Old and New Testaments together as <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">sacred scripture</a>. The New Testament (in whole or in part) has frequently accompanied the <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">spread of Christianity around the world</a>. It reflects and serves as a source for <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_ethics" title="Christian ethics">morality</a>. Both extended readings and phrases directly from the New Testament are also incorporated (along with readings from the Old Testament) into the various <a href="/wiki/Christian_liturgy" title="Christian liturgy">Christian liturgies</a>. The New Testament has influenced religious, <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_movement" title="Philosophical movement">philosophical</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Political_movement" title="Political movement">political</a> <a href="/wiki/Social_movement" title="Social movement">movements</a> in <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> and left an indelible mark on literature, art, and music.</p>
<p>The New Testament is a collection of Christian works written in the <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">common (Koine) Greek</a> language of the first century, at different times by various writers, and the modern consensus is that it also provides important evidence regarding <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_Judaism" title="Christianity and Judaism">Judaism</a> in the first century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In almost all Christian traditions today, the New Testament consists of 27 books. The original texts were written in the first and perhaps the second centuries of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Era" title="Christian Era">Christian Era</a>, in Greek, which was the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">common language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">Conquests of Alexander the Great</a> (335–323 BC) until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a> in the 7th century AD. All the works that eventually became incorporated into the New Testament are believed to have been written no later than around 120 AD,.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seeStandard_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-seeStandard-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="this source is not specific. If referencing a book, more information should be given. (August 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Robinson_(bishop_of_Woolwich)#Redating_the_New_Testament_.281976.29" title="John Robinson (bishop of Woolwich)">John A. T. Robinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_B._Wallace" title="Daniel B. Wallace">Dan Wallace</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_F._Albright" title="William F. Albright">William F. Albright</a> dated all the books of the New Testament before 70 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Others give a final date of 80 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> or at 96 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mounce_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mounce-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Collections of related texts such as <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">letters</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Apostle Paul</a> (a major collection of which must have been made already by the early 2nd century)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canonical_Gospels" title="Canonical Gospels">Canonical Gospels</a> of Matthew, Mark, Luke, and John (asserted by <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus of Lyon</a> in the late-2nd century as <i>the</i> Four Gospels) gradually were joined to other collections and single works in different combinations to <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_Christian_biblical_canon" title="Development of the Christian biblical canon">form various Christian canons of Scripture</a>. Over time, some <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">disputed books</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_epistles" title="General epistles">Minor Catholic (General) Epistles</a> were introduced into <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">canons</a> in which they were originally absent. Other works earlier held to be Scripture, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_Clement" title="1 Clement">1 Clement</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shepherd_of_Hermas" title="Shepherd of Hermas">Shepherd of Hermas</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Diatessaron" title="Diatessaron">Diatessaron</a>, were excluded from the New Testament. The Old Testament canon is not completely uniform among all major Christian groups including <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestants</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_Orthodox" title="Slavic Orthodox">Slavic Orthodox Churches</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_Orthodox_Church" title="Armenian Orthodox Church">Armenian Orthodox Church</a>. However, the <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">twenty-seven-book canon</a> of the New Testament, at least since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, has been almost universally recognized within <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">Development of the New Testament canon</a>).</p>
<p>The New Testament consists of:</p>
<ul>
<li>Four narratives of the life, teaching, death and resurrection of Jesus called "gospel" or the good news.</li>
<li>A narrative of the <a href="/wiki/Apostles" title="Apostles">Apostle</a> ministries in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early church</a>, called the "Acts of the Apostles", and probably written by the same writer as the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a>, which it continues;</li>
<li>Twenty-one letters, often called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistles" title="Epistles">epistles</a>" from Greek "epistole", written by various authors, and consisting of Christian doctrine, counsel, instruction, and conflict resolution; and</li>
<li>An <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse" title="Apocalypse">Apocalypse</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">book of prophecy</a>, containing some instructions to <a href="/wiki/Seven_churches_of_Asia" title="Seven churches of Asia">seven local congregations of Asia Minor</a>, but mostly containing prophetical <a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">symbology</a>, about the <a href="/wiki/End_time" title="End time">end times</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>The term "new testament" (<a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>: Ἡ Καινὴ Διαθήκη, <i>Hē Kainḕ Diathḗkē</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (February 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_covenant" title="New covenant">new covenant</a>" (Hebrew בְּרִית חֲדָשָׁה <i>bərîṯ ḥăḏāšâ</i>)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> first occurs in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jeremiah+31%3A31&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Jeremiah 31:31</a></span> (Greek <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> καινὴ διαθήκη <i>kainḕ diathḗkē</i>, cited in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Hebrews+8%3A8&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Hebrews 8:8</a></span>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The same Greek phrase for "new covenant" is found elsewhere in the New Testament (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+22%3A20&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 22:20</a></span>, <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1cor+11%3A25&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">1 Corinthians 11:25</a></span>, <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2cor+3%3A6&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">2 Corinthians 3:6</a></span>, <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Hebrews+8%3A8&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Hebrews 8:8</a></span>, and <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Hebrews+9%3A15&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Hebrews 9:15</a></span>; cf. <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2Cor+3%3A14&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">2 Corinthians 3:14</a></span>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (February 2016)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In early <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Latin" title="Bible translations into Latin">Bible translations into Latin</a>, the phrase was rendered <i>foedus</i>, "federation", in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jeremiah+31%3A31&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Jeremiah 31:31</a></span>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2016)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and was rendered <i>testamentum</i> in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Hebrews+8%3A8&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Hebrews 8:8</a></span> and other instances from which comes the English term "New Testament."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Modern English, like Latin, distinguishes <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/testament" title="wikt:testament">testament</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/covenant" title="wikt:covenant">covenant</a> as alternative translations, and consequently the treatment of the term διαθήκη <i>diathḗkē</i> varies in <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_English" title="Bible translations into English">Bible translations into English</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>'s 1395 version is a translation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Vulgate" title="Latin Vulgate">Latin Vulgate</a> and so follows different terms in Jeremiah and Hebrews:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<blockquote>
<dl>
<dd>Lo! days shall come, saith the Lord, and I shall make a new covenant (from Latin <i>foedus</i>) with the house of Israel, and with the house of Judah.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jeremiah+31%3A31&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Jeremiah 31:31]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>For he reproving him saith, Lo! days come, saith the Lord, when I shall establish a new testament (from Latin <i>testamentum</i>) on the house of Israel, and on the house of Judah.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Hebrews+8%3A8&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Hebrews 8:8]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
</blockquote>
<p>Use of the term <i>New Testament</i> to describe a collection of first and second-century Christian Greek Scriptures can be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> (in <i>Against <a href="/wiki/Praxeas" title="Praxeas">Praxeas</a></i> 15).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In <i>Against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcion" title="Marcion">Marcion</a></i>, written <i>circa</i> 208 AD, he writes of</p>
<blockquote>
<p>the Divine Word, who is doubly edged with the two testaments of the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">law</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">gospel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>And Tertullian continues later in the book, writing:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>it is certain that the whole aim at which he [Marcion] has strenuously laboured, even in the drawing up of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antitheses" title="Antitheses">Antitheses</a>, centres in this, that he may establish a diversity between the Old and the New Testaments, so that his own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a> may be separate from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creator_God" title="Creator God">Creator</a>, as belonging to this rival god, and as alien from the law and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neviim" title="Neviim">prophets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[note 1]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>By the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century" title="Christianity in the 4th century">4th century</a>, the existence—even if not the exact contents—of both an Old and New Testament had been established. <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a>, a 3rd–4th century Christian author wrote in his early-4th-century Latin <i>Institutiones Divinae</i> (<i>Divine Institutes</i>):</p>
<blockquote>
<p>But all scripture is divided into two Testaments. That which preceded the advent and passion of Christ—that is, the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">law</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neviim" title="Neviim">prophets</a>—is called the Old; but those things which were written after His resurrection are named the New Testament. The Jews make use of the Old, we of the New: but yet they are not discordant, for the New is the fulfilling of the Old, and in both there is the same testator, even Christ, who, having suffered death for us, made us heirs of His everlasting kingdom, the people of the Jews being deprived and disinherited. As the prophet Jeremiah testifies when he speaks such things: "Behold, the days come, saith the Lord, that I will make a new testament to the house of Israel and the house of Judah, not according to the testament which I made to their fathers, in the day that I took them by the hand to bring them out of the land of Egypt; for they continued not in my testament, and I disregarded them, saith the Lord."<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jer+31%3A31%E2%80%9332&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Jer 31:31–32]</a></sup> ... For that which He said above, that He would make a new testament to the house of Judah, shows that the old testament which was given by Moses was not perfect; but that which was to be given by Christ would be complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Books">Books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(February 2016)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_biblical_canons" title="Christian biblical canons">Christian biblical canons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">Development of the New Testament canon</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">New Testament apocrypha</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Template:Books_of_the_New_Testament" title="Template:Books of the New Testament">Template:Books of the New Testament</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canon_of_the_New_Testament" title="Canon of the New Testament">canon of the New Testament</a> is the collection of books that most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> regard as <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">divinely inspired</a> and constituting the New Testament of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_biblical_canon" title="Christian biblical canon">Christian Biblical Canon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcdowell2006_canon_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcdowell2006_canon-14">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the period extending roughly from 50 to 150 AD, a number of documents began to circulate among the churches, including epistles, gospel accounts, memoirs, prophecies, homilies, and collections of teachings.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> While some of these documents were <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Age" title="Apostolic Age">apostolic in origin</a>, others drew upon the tradition the apostles and ministers of the word had utilized in their individual missions, and still others represented a summation of the teaching entrusted to a particular church center.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Several of these writings sought to extend, interpret, and apply apostolic teaching to meet the needs of Christians in a given locality.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In general, among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" title="Christian denominations">Christian denominations</a> the New Testament canon came to be agreed-upon as a list of 27 books, although the order of the books can vary from one version of the printed scriptures to the next.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The book order is the same in the Greek Orthodox,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Roman Catholic,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and Protestant traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luther_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luther-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (February 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavonic_Orthodox" title="Slavonic Orthodox">Slavonic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_Orthodox_Church" title="Armenian Orthodox Church">Armenian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian</a> traditions have different New Testament book orders.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Gospels">The Gospels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The Gospels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canonical_gospels" title="Canonical gospels">Canonical gospels</a></div>
<p>Each of the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gospels" title="Gospels">gospels</a> in the New Testament narrates the life, death, and resurrection of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nazareth" title="Nazareth">Nazareth</a>. The word "gospel" derives from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_English_language" title="Old English language">Old English</a> <i>gōd-spell</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> (rarely <i>godspel</i>), meaning "good news" or "glad tidings". The gospel was considered the "good news" of the coming <a href="/wiki/Messiah#Christianity" title="Messiah">Kingdom of Messiah</a>, and the redemption through the life and death of Jesus, the central Christian message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODCC_self_Gospel_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC_self_Gospel-17">[16]</a></sup> Gospel is a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> (word-for-word translation) of the <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a> word <span lang="grc">εὐαγγέλιον</span>, <i>euangelion</i> (<i>eu-</i> "good", <i>-angelion</i> "message").</p>
<p>Since the 2nd century, the four narrative accounts of the life and work of Jesus Christ have been referred to as "The Gospel of ..." or "The Gospel according to ..." followed by the name of the supposed author. Whatever these admittedly early ascriptions may imply about the sources behind or the perception of these gospels, they are anonymous compositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seeStandard_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-seeStandard-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a>, ascribed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matthew_the_Evangelist" title="Matthew the Evangelist">Apostle Matthew</a>. This gospel begins with a <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_of_Jesus" title="Genealogy of Jesus">genealogy of Jesus</a> and a story of his birth that includes a visit from <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Magi" title="Biblical Magi">magi</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Flight_into_Egypt" title="Flight into Egypt">flight into Egypt</a>, and it ends with the <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">commissioning of the disciples</a> by the resurrected Jesus.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Gospel of Mark</a>, ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Mark_the_Evangelist" title="Mark the Evangelist">Mark the Evangelist</a>. This gospel begins with the preaching of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">baptism of Jesus</a>. Two different secondary endings were affixed to this gospel in the 2nd century.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a>, ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Luke_the_Evangelist" title="Luke the Evangelist">Luke the Evangelist</a>, who was not one of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_Disciples#The_Twelve_Apostles" title="Twelve Disciples">the Twelve Apostles</a>, but was mentioned as a companion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus">Apostle Paul</a> and as a physician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> This gospel begins with parallel stories of the birth and childhood of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> and ends with appearances of the resurrected Jesus and his ascension into heaven.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>, ascribed to <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a>. This gospel begins with a philosophical prologue and ends with appearances of the resurrected Jesus, it is about Jesus's miracles.</li>
</ul>
<p>The first three gospels listed above are classified as the <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_Gospels" title="Synoptic Gospels">Synoptic Gospels</a>. They contain similar accounts of the events in Jesus' life and his teaching, due to their literary interdependence. The Gospel of John is structured differently and includes stories of several miracles of Jesus and sayings not found in the other three.</p>
<p>These four gospels that were eventually included in the New Testament were only a few among many other early Christian gospels. The existence of such texts is even mentioned at the beginning of the Gospel of Luke.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+1%3A1%E2%80%934&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Luke 1:1–4]</a></sup> Other early Christian gospels such as the so-called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish-Christian_Gospels" title="Jewish-Christian Gospels">Jewish-Christian Gospels</a>" or the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Thomas" title="Gospel of Thomas">Gospel of Thomas</a>, also offer both a window into the context of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christianity</a> and may provide some assistance in the reconstruction of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">historical Jesus</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts_of_the_Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Acts of the Apostles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a> is a narrative of the apostles' ministry and activity after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ%27s_death" title="Christ's death">Christ's death</a> and resurrection, from which point it resumes and functions as a sequel to the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a>. Examining style, phraseology, and other evidence, modern scholarship generally concludes that Acts and the Gospel of Luke share the same author, referred to as <a href="/wiki/Luke%E2%80%93Acts" title="Luke–Acts">Luke–Acts</a>. Luke-Acts does not name its author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurkett2002196_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurkett2002196-19">[18]</a></sup> Church tradition identified him as <a href="/wiki/Luke_the_Evangelist" title="Luke the Evangelist">Luke the Evangelist</a>, the companion of Paul, but the majority of scholars reject this due to the many contradictions between Acts and the authentic Pauline letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2005235_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2005235-20">[19]</a></sup> The most probable date of composition is around 80–100 AD, and there is evidence that it was still being substantially revised well into the 2nd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerkins2009_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerkins2009-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistles">Epistles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Epistles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The epistles of the New Testament are considered by Christians to be divinely inspired and holy letters, written by the apostles and disciples of Christ, to either local congregations with specific needs, or to New Covenant Christians in general, scattered about; or "General Epistles."</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pauline_Letters_to_Churches">Pauline Letters to Churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Pauline Letters to Churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">Pauline epistles</a></div>
<p>The Pauline epistles are the thirteen New Testament books that present <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a> as their author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[note 2]</a></sup> Six of the letters are disputed. Four are thought by most modern scholars to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudepigraphy" title="Pseudepigraphy">pseudepigraphic</a>, i.e., not actually written by Paul even if attributed to him within the letters themselves. Opinion is more divided on the other two disputed letters (2 Thessalonians and Colossians).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> These letters were written to Christian communities in specific cities or geographical regions, often to address issues faced by that particular community. Prominent themes include the relationship both to broader "<a href="/wiki/Gentile" title="Gentile">pagan</a>" society, to Judaism, and to other Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Romans" title="Epistle to the Romans">Epistle to the Romans</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians" title="First Epistle to the Corinthians">First Epistle to the Corinthians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians" title="Second Epistle to the Corinthians">Second Epistle to the Corinthians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Galatians" title="Epistle to the Galatians">Epistle to the Galatians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Ephesians" title="Epistle to the Ephesians">Epistle to the Ephesians</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Philippians" title="Epistle to the Philippians">Epistle to the Philippians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Colossians" title="Epistle to the Colossians">Epistle to the Colossians</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_the_Thessalonians" title="First Epistle to the Thessalonians">First Epistle to the Thessalonians</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_to_the_Thessalonians" title="Second Epistle to the Thessalonians">Second Epistle to the Thessalonians</a><sup>*</sup></li>
</ul>
<p>[Disputed letters are marked with an asterisk (*).]</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pauline_Letters_to_Persons">Pauline Letters to Persons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pauline Letters to Persons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The last four Pauline letters in the New Testament are addressed to individual persons. They include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_Timothy" title="First Epistle to Timothy">First Epistle to Timothy</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_to_Timothy" title="Second Epistle to Timothy">Second Epistle to Timothy</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_Titus" title="Epistle to Titus">Epistle to Titus</a><sup>*</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_Philemon" title="Epistle to Philemon">Epistle to Philemon</a></li>
</ul>
<p>[Disputed letters are marked with an asterisk (*).]</p>
<p>All of the above except for Philemon are known as the <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_epistles" title="Pastoral epistles">Pastoral epistles</a>. They are addressed to individuals charged with pastoral oversight of churches and discuss issues of Christian living, doctrine and leadership. They often address different concerns to those of the preceding epistles. These letters are believed by many to be pseudepigraphic. Some scholars (e.g., Bill Mounce, Ben Witherington) will argue that the letters are genuinely Pauline, or at least written under Paul's supervision.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hebrews">Hebrews</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hebrews">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Hebrews" title="Epistle to the Hebrews">Epistle to the Hebrews</a> addresses a Jewish audience who had come to believe that Jesus was the <a href="/wiki/Messiah#Judaism" title="Messiah">anointed one</a> (Hebrew: מָשִׁיחַ—transliterated in English as "Moshiach", or "Messiah"; Greek: Χριστός—transliterated in English as "Christos", for "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a>") who was predicted in the writings of the Hebrew Bible. The author discusses the "better-ness" of the new covenant and the ministry of Jesus, over the Mosaic covenant <sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Heb.+1%3A1%E2%80%9310%3A18&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Heb. 1:1–10:18]</a></sup> and urges the readers in the practical implications of this conviction through the end of the epistle.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Heb.+10%3A19%E2%80%9313%3A25&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Heb. 10:19–13:25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The book has been widely accepted by the Christian church as inspired by God and thus authoritative, despite the acknowledgment of uncertainties about who its human author was. Regarding authorship, although the Epistle to the Hebrews does not internally claim to have been written by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus">Apostle Paul</a>, some similarities in wordings to some of the Pauline Epistles have been noted and inferred. In antiquity, some began to ascribe it to Paul in an attempt to provide the anonymous work an explicit apostolic pedigree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 4th century, <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> supported <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Pauline_epistles" title="Authorship of the Pauline epistles">Paul's authorship</a>. The Church largely agreed to include Hebrews as the fourteenth letter of Paul, and affirmed this authorship until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>. The letter to the Hebrews had difficulty in being accepted as part of the Christian canon because of its anonymity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> As early as the 3rd century, <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> wrote of the letter, "Men of old have handed it down as Paul's, but who wrote the Epistle God only knows."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Contemporary scholars often reject Pauline authorship for the epistle to the Hebrews,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrman_2004:411_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman_2004:411-29">[27]</a></sup> based on its distinctive style and theology, which are considered to set it apart from Paul's writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Powell-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="General_epistles">General epistles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: General epistles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_epistles" title="General epistles">General epistles</a> (or "catholic epistles") consist of both letters and treatises in the form of letters written to the church at large. The term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">catholic</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a>: καθολική, <i>katholikē</i>), used to describe these letters in the oldest manuscripts containing them, here simply means "general" or "universal". The authorship of a number of these is disputed.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a>, written by an author named "James", often identified with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_the_Just" title="James the Just">James, the brother of Jesus</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_Peter" title="First Epistle of Peter">First Epistle of Peter</a>, ascribed to the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Apostle Peter</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_of_Peter" title="Second Epistle of Peter">Second Epistle of Peter</a>, ascribed to the Apostle Peter, though widely considered not to have been written by him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_John" title="First Epistle of John">First Epistle of John</a>, ascribed to <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_of_John" title="Second Epistle of John">Second Epistle of John</a>, ascribed to John the Apostle.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Third_Epistle_of_John" title="Third Epistle of John">Third Epistle of John</a>, ascribed to John the Apostle.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Jude" title="Epistle of Jude">Epistle of Jude</a>, written under the name of <a href="/wiki/Jude,_brother_of_Jesus" title="Jude, brother of Jesus">Jude, the brother of Jesus and James</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Book_of_Revelation">Book of Revelation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Book of Revelation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Johannine_works" title="Authorship of the Johannine works">Authorship of the Johannine works</a></div>
<p>The final book of the New Testament is the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a>, also known as the Apocalypse of John. In the New Testament canon, it is considered <a href="/wiki/Bible_prophecy" title="Bible prophecy">prophetical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptic_literature" title="Apocalyptic literature">apocalyptic literature</a>. Its authorship has been attributed either to John the Apostle (in which case it is often thought that John the Apostle is <a href="/wiki/John_the_Evangelist" title="John the Evangelist">John the Evangelist</a>, i.e. author of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>) or to another John designated "<a href="/wiki/John_of_Patmos" title="John of Patmos">John of Patmos</a>" after the island where the text says the revelation was received (1:9). Some ascribe the writership date as circa 81–96 AD, and others at around 68 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mounce_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mounce-6">[6]</a></sup> The work opens with letters to <a href="/wiki/Seven_churches_of_Asia" title="Seven churches of Asia">seven churches</a> and thereafter takes the form of an <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse" title="Apocalypse">apocalypse</a>, a literary genre popular in ancient Judaism and Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Testament_canons">New Testament canons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: New Testament canons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<dt>Table notes</dt>

<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Armenian-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Armenian_34-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The growth and development of the Armenian Biblical canon is complex; extra-canonical New Testament books appear in historical canon lists and recensions that are either distinct to this tradition, or where they do exist elsewhere, never achieved the same status.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some of the books are not listed in this table; these include the Prayer of <a href="/wiki/Euthalius" title="Euthalius">Euthalius</a>, the Repose of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._John_the_Evangelist" title="St. John the Evangelist">St. John the Evangelist</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_Addai" title="Doctrine of Addai">Doctrine of Addai</a>, a reading from the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_James" title="Gospel of James">Gospel of James</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostolic_Canons" title="Apostolic Canons">the Second Apostolic Canons</a>, the Words of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Barsabbas" title="Joseph Barsabbas">Justus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Dionysius_the_Areopagite" title="Pseudo-Dionysius the Areopagite">Dionysius Aeropagite</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preaching_of_Peter" title="Preaching of Peter">Preaching of Peter</a>, and a Poem by <a href="/wiki/Ghazar_Parpetsi" title="Ghazar Parpetsi">Ghazar</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> (Various sources<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> also mention undefined Armenian canonical additions to the Gospels of Mark and John, however, these may refer to the general additions—Mark 16:9–20 and John 7:53–8:11—discussed elsewhere in these notes.) A possible exception here to canonical exclusivity is the Second Apostolic Canons, which share a common source—the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Constitutions" title="Apostolic Constitutions">Apostolic Constitutions</a>—with certain parts of the Orthodox Tewahedo New Testament broader canon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <i>Acts of Thaddeus</i> was included in the biblical canon of <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tatev" title="Gregory of Tatev">Gregory of Tatev</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENersessian200129_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENersessian200129-33">[31]</a></sup> There is some uncertainty about whether Armenian canon lists include the Doctrine of Addai or the related <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Thaddeus" title="Acts of Thaddeus">Acts of Thaddeus</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Moreover, the correspondence between King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agbar" title="Agbar">Agbar</a> and Jesus Christ, which is found in various forms—including within both the Doctrine of Addai and the Acts of Thaddeus—sometimes appears separately (see <a class="external text" href="http://www.looys.net/BIBCANON.DOC" rel="nofollow">list</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (February 2016)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>). The Prayer of Euthalius and the Repose of St. John the Evangelist appear in the appendix of the 1805 Armenian Zohrab Bible;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> however, some of the aforementioned books, though they are found within canon lists, have nonetheless never been discovered to be part of any Armenian Biblical manuscript.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENersessian200129_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENersessian200129-33">[31]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-infancy-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-infancy_35-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Though widely regarded as non-canonical,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the Gospel of James obtained early liturgical acceptance among some Eastern churches and remains a major source for many of Christendom's traditions related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary,_the_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, the mother of Jesus">Mary, the mother of Jesus</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Tatian-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Tatian_36-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Tatian_36-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Tatian_36-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Tatian_36-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Diatessaron" title="Diatessaron">Diatessaron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatian" title="Tatian">Tatian</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Gospel_harmony" title="Gospel harmony">gospel harmony</a>, became a standard text in some Syriac-speaking churches down to the 5th century, when it gave-way to the four separate gospels found in the Peshitta.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Addition-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Addition_37-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Addition_37-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Addition_37-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Addition_37-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Parts of these four books are not found in the most reliable ancient sources; in some cases, are thought to be later additions, and have therefore not appeared historically in every Biblical tradition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> They are as follows: <a href="/wiki/Mark_16" title="Mark 16">Mark 16:9–20</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesus_and_the_woman_taken_in_adultery" title="Jesus and the woman taken in adultery">John 7:53–8:11</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Comma_Johanneum" title="Comma Johanneum">Comma Johanneum</a>, and portions of the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles#Manuscripts" title="Acts of the Apostles">Western version of Acts</a>. To varying degrees, arguments for the authenticity of these passages—especially for the one from the Gospel of John—have occasionally been made.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Goth-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Goth_38-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Skeireins" title="Skeireins">Skeireins</a>, a commentary on the Gospel of John in the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic language</a>, was included in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wulfila_Bible" title="Wulfila Bible">Wulfila Bible</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It exists today only in fragments.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-AoP-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AoP_39-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AoP_39-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The Acts of Paul and Thecla, the Epistle of the Corinthians to Paul, and the Third Epistle to the Corinthians are all portions of the greater <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Paul" title="Acts of Paul">Acts of Paul</a> narrative, which is part of a stichometric catalogue of New Testament canon found in the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Claromontanus" title="Codex Claromontanus">Codex Claromontanus</a>, but has survived only in fragments.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some of the content within these individual sections may have developed separately.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Luther-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Luther_42-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Luther_42-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Luther_42-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Luther_42-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">These four works were questioned or "<a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">spoken against</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, and he changed the order of <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">his New Testament</a> to reflect this, but he did not leave them out, nor has any <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> body since.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Traditional German Luther Bibles are still printed with the New Testament in this changed "Lutheran" order.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The vast majority of Protestants embrace these four works as fully canonical.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Syriac-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_43-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_43-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_43-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_43-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Syriac_43-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The Peshitta excludes 2 John, 3 John, 2 Peter, Jude, and Revelation, but certain Bibles of the modern Syriac traditions include later translations of those books.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Still today, the official <a href="/wiki/Lectionary" title="Lectionary">lectionary</a> followed by the Syriac Orthodox Church and the Assyrian Church of the East presents lessons from only the twenty-two books of Peshitta, the version to which appeal is made for the settlement of doctrinal questions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Corinthians-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Corinthians_44-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Third Epistle to the Corinthians often appears with and is framed as a response to the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_the_Corinthians_to_Paul" title="Epistle of the Corinthians to Paul">Epistle of the Corinthians to Paul</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Laodiceans-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Laodiceans_45-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Epistle to the Laodiceans is present in some western non-Roman Catholic translations and traditions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Especially of note is <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>'s inclusion of the epistle in his English translation,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a>' use of it to the point where they produced a translation and made pleas for its canonicity, see Poole's <i>Annotations</i>, on Col. 4:16. The epistle is nonetheless widely rejected by the vast majority of Protestants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-ApocPeter-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ApocPeter_47-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse_of_Peter" title="Apocalypse of Peter">Apocalypse of Peter</a>, though not listed in this table, is mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Muratorian_fragment" title="Muratorian fragment">Muratorian fragment</a> and is part of a stichometric catalogue of New Testament canon found in the Codex Claromontanus.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It was also held in high regard by <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Fathers-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Fathers_48-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other known writings of the Apostolic Fathers not listed in this table are as follows: the seven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistles_of_Ignatius" title="Epistles of Ignatius">Epistles of Ignatius</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Polycarp" title="Epistle of Polycarp">Epistle of Polycarp</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Martyrdom_of_Polycarp" title="Martyrdom of Polycarp">Martyrdom of Polycarp</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_Diognetus" title="Epistle to Diognetus">Epistle to Diognetus</a>, the fragment of <a href="/wiki/Quadratus_of_Athens" title="Quadratus of Athens">Quadratus of Athens</a>, the fragments of <a href="/wiki/Papias_of_Hierapolis" title="Papias of Hierapolis">Papias of Hierapolis</a>, the Reliques of the Elders Preserved in <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles' Creed">Apostles' Creed</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Apostloic-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Apostloic_49-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Though they are not listed in this table, the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Constitutions" title="Apostolic Constitutions">Apostolic Constitutions</a> were considered canonical by some including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexius_Aristenus" title="Alexius Aristenus">Alexius Aristenus</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_of_Salisbury" title="John of Salisbury">John of Salisbury</a>, and to a lesser extent, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grigor_Tatevatsi" title="Grigor Tatevatsi">Grigor Tat`evatsi</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> They are even classified as part of the New Testament canon within the body of the Constitutions itself; moreover, they are the source for a great deal of the content in the Orthodox Tewahedo broader canon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-ApFa-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_50-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_50-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_50-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_50-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ApFa_50-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">These five writings attributed to the Apostolic Fathers are not currently considered canonical in any Biblical tradition, though they are more highly regarded by some more than others.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Nonetheless, their early authorship and inclusion in ancient Biblical codices, as well as their acceptance to varying degrees by various early authorities, requires them to be treated as foundational literature for Christianity as a whole.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2016)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Ethiopic-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Ethiopic_51-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ethiopic_51-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Ethiopic Clement and the Ethiopic Didascalia are distinct from and should not be confused with other ecclesiastical documents known in the west by similar names.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Book_order">Book order</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Book order">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The order in which the books of the New Testament appear differs between some collections and ecclesiastical traditions. In the Latin West, prior to the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> (an early 5th-century Latin version of the Bible), the four Gospels were arranged in the following order: Matthew, John, Luke, and Mark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[note 3]</a></sup> The Syriac <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a> places the major General epistles (James, 1 Peter, and 1 John) immediately after Acts and before the Pauline epistles.</p>
<p>The order of an early edition of the letters of Paul is based on the size of the letters: longest to shortest, though keeping 1 and 2 Corinthians and 1 and 2 Thessalonians together. The Pastoral epistles were apparently not part of the <i>Corpus Paulinum</i> in which this order originated and were later inserted after 2 Thessalonians and before Philemon. Hebrews was variously incorporated into the <i>Corpus Paulinum</i> either after 2 Thessalonians, after Philemon (i.e. at the very end), or after Romans.</p>
<p>The New Testament of the 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Luther Bible</a> continues, to this day, to place Hebrews, James, Jude, and the Apocalypse last. This reflects the thoughts of the Reformer Martin Luther <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_canon" title="Luther's canon">on the canonicity of these books</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[note 4]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Apocrypha">Apocrypha</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Apocrypha">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">New Testament apocrypha</a></div>
<p>The books that eventually found a permanent place in the New Testament were not the only works of Christian literature produced in the earliest Christian centuries. The long process of <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">canonization</a> began early, sometimes with tacit reception of traditional texts, sometimes with explicit selection or rejection of particular texts as either acceptable or unacceptable for use in a given context (e.g., not all texts that were acceptable for private use were considered appropriate for use in the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a>).</p>
<p>Over the course of history, those works of early Christian literature that survived but that did not become part of the New Testament have been variously grouped by theologians and scholars. Drawing upon, though redefining, an older term used in <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christianity</a> and among Protestants when referring to those books found in the Christian <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> although not in the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Jewish Bible</a>, modern scholars began to refer to these works of early Christian literature not included in the New Testament as "apocryphal", by which was meant non-canonical.</p>
<p>Collected editions of these works were then referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">New Testament apocrypha</a>". Typically excluded from such published collections are the following groups of works: <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Fathers" title="Apostolic Fathers">The Apostolic Fathers</a>, the 2nd-century Christian apologists, <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_school" title="Alexandrian school">the Alexandrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methodius_of_Olympus" title="Methodius of Olympus">Methodius of Olympus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novatian" title="Novatian">Novatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprian" title="Cyprian">Cyprian</a>, martyrdoms, and <a href="/wiki/Desert_Fathers" title="Desert Fathers">the Desert Fathers</a>. Almost all other Christian literature from the period, and sometimes including works composed well into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, are relegated to the so-called New Testament apocrypha.</p>
<p>Although not considered to be inspired by God, these "apocryphal" works may be helpful in the study of the New Testament in that they were produced in the same ancient context and often using the same language as those books that would eventually form the New Testament. Some of these later works are dependent (either directly or indirectly) upon books that would later come to be in the New Testament or upon the ideas expressed in them. There is even an example of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudepigraph" title="Pseudepigraph">pseudepigraphical</a> letter composed under the guise of a presumably lost letter of the Apostle Paul, the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Laodiceans" title="Epistle to the Laodiceans">Epistle to the Laodiceans</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Authors">Authors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Authors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Bible" title="Authorship of the Bible">Authorship of the Bible</a></div>
