<p>The books of the New Testament were all or nearly all written by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Christians" title="Jewish Christians">Jewish Christians</a>—that is, Jewish disciples of Christ, who lived in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, and under <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Roman occupation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[36]</a></sup> Luke, who wrote the Gospel of Luke and the Book of Acts, is frequently thought of as an exception; scholars are divided as to whether Luke was a Gentile or a <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Jew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strelan2013_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strelan2013-56">[37]</a></sup> A few scholars identify the author of the Gospel of Mark as probably a Gentile, and similarly for the Gospel of Matthew, though most assert Jewish-Christian authorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[40]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (February 2016)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gospels">Gospels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gospels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_Gospels" title="Synoptic Gospels">Synoptic Gospels</a></div>
<p>Authorship is an area of longstanding and current research and debate, with different works posing different problems for identification. While the various works have traditional ascriptions of authorship, these ascriptions are in some cases defended by scholars, and in other cases disputed or rejected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[41]</a></sup> According to many (if not most) critical scholars, none of the authors of the Gospels were eyewitnesses or even explicitly claimed to be eyewitnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lost_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-lost-61">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carolina2004_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carolina2004-62">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iuda_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-iuda-63">[44]</a></sup> Bart Ehrman of the University of North Carolina has argued for a scholarly consensus that many New Testament books were not written by the individuals whose names are attached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iuda_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-iuda-63">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[45]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (February 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He further argues that the Gospels were originally anonymous, and names were not ascribed to them until around 185 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[47]</a></sup> Other scholars concur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickle2001_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nickle2001-68">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witherington2004_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witherington2004-69">[50]</a></sup> It is the perspective of some writers that none were written in Palestine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[51]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (January 2016)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>There is a tradition that the Apostle John was the author of the Gospel of John. Traditionalists (such as Biblical commentators Albert Barnes and Matthew Henry) claim that the writer of the Gospel of John himself claimed to be an eyewitness in their commentaries of John 21:24 and therefore the gospel was written by an eyewitness;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henry-72">[53]</a></sup> however, this idea is rejected by the majority of modern scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>A review of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bauckham" title="Richard Bauckham">Richard Bauckham</a>'s book <i>Jesus and the Eyewitnesses: The Gospels as Eyewitness Testimony</i> states "The common wisdom in the academy is that stories and sayings of Jesus circulated for decades, undergoing countless retellings and embellishments before being finally set down in writing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HahnScott2007_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-HahnScott2007-74">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most scholars hold to the <a href="/wiki/Two-source_hypothesis" title="Two-source hypothesis">two-source hypothesis</a>, which claims that the <a href="/wiki/Marcan_priority" title="Marcan priority">Gospel of Mark was written first</a>. According to the hypothesis, the authors of the Gospel of Matthew and the Gospel of Luke then used the Gospel of Mark and the hypothetical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Q_document" title="Q document">Q document</a>, in addition to some other sources, to write their individual gospel accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[60]</a></sup> These three gospels are called the Synoptic gospels because they include many of the same stories, often in the same sequence, and sometimes exactly the same wording. Scholars agree that the Gospel of John was written last, by using a different tradition and body of testimony. In addition, most scholars agree that the author of Luke wrote the Acts of the Apostles. Scholars hold that these books constituted two halves of a single work, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luke-Acts" title="Luke-Acts">Luke-Acts</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1545" data-file-width="1210" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg/220px-The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg/330px-The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg/440px-The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Evangelist_Matthew_Inspired_by_an_Angel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Evangelist Mathäus und der Engel</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Strictly speaking, each gospel and the book of Acts is arguably anonymous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-80">[61]</a></sup> The Gospel of John is somewhat of an exception, although the author simply refers to himself as "the disciple Jesus loved" and claims to be a member of Jesus' inner circle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_John_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris_John-81">[62]</a></sup> The identities of each author were agreed upon at an early date, certainly no later than the early 2nd century. It is likely that the issue of the authorship of each gospel had been settled at least somewhat earlier,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[63]</a></sup> as the earliest sources are in complete agreement on the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donald_Guthrie_1990_pp._37-40_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donald_Guthrie_1990_pp._37-40-83">[64]</a></sup> No one questioned the early 2nd century consensus until the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donald_Guthrie_1990_pp._37-40_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Donald_Guthrie_1990_pp._37-40-83">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some scholars today maintain the traditional claim that <a href="/wiki/Luke_the_Evangelist" title="Luke the Evangelist">Luke the Evangelist</a>, an associate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Paul" title="St. Paul">St. Paul</a> who was probably not an eyewitness to Jesus' ministry, wrote the Gospel of Luke and Acts of the Apostles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donald_Guthrie_1990_pp._37-40_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Donald_Guthrie_1990_pp._37-40-83">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H._Marshall.2C_1980_pp._44-45_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-H._Marshall.2C_1980_pp._44-45-84">[65]</a></sup> Scholars are also divided on the traditional claim that <a href="/wiki/Mark_the_Evangelist" title="Mark the Evangelist">Mark the Evangelist</a>, an associate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Peter" title="St. Peter">St. Peter</a> who may have been an eyewitness to Jesus' ministry, wrote the Gospel of Mark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernd_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernd-85">[66]</a></sup> Scholars are more divided over the traditional claim that Matthew the Apostle wrote the Gospel of Matthew<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[68]</a></sup> and that John the Apostle wrote the Gospel of John.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODCC_self_John_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC_self_John-88">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newadvent.org_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-newadvent.org-89">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[71]</a></sup> Opinion, however, is widely divided on this issue and there is no widespread consensus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown164_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown164-91">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts">Acts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Acts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_Luke%E2%80%93Acts" title="Authorship of Luke–Acts">Authorship of Luke–Acts</a></div>
<p>The Gospel of Luke and the Acts of the Apostles were both written by the same author, and are thus referred to as the Lucan texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[75]</a></sup> The most direct evidence comes from the prefaces of each book; both were addressed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theophilus_(Biblical)" title="Theophilus (Biblical)">Theophilus</a>, and the preface to the Acts of the Apostles references "my former book" about the ministry of Jesus.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Furthermore, there are linguistic and theological similarities between the two works, suggesting that they have a common author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pauline_epistles">Pauline epistles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pauline epistles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Pauline_epistles" title="Authorship of the Pauline epistles">Authorship of the Pauline epistles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="599" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/220px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/330px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/440px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Saint Paul Writing His Epistles</i>, 17th-century painting. Most scholars think Paul actually dictated his letters to a secretary.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Pauline epistles are the thirteen books in the New Testament traditionally attributed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul of Tarsus</a>. The anonymous Epistle to the Hebrews is, despite unlikely Pauline authorship, often functionally grouped with these thirteen to form a corpus of fourteen "Pauline" epistles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[note 5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Seven letters are generally classified as "undisputed", expressing contemporary scholarly near consensus that they are the work of Paul: Romans, 1 Corinthians, 2 Corinthians, Galatians, Philippians, 1 Thessalonians and Philemon. Six additional letters bearing Paul's name do not currently enjoy the same academic consensus: Ephesians, Colossians, 2 Thessalonians, 1 Timothy, 2 Timothy and Titus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lock.2C_Meinertz_p.622_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lock.2C_Meinertz_p.622-101">[note 6]</a></sup></p>
<p>While many scholars uphold the traditional view, some question whether the first three, called the "Deutero-Pauline Epistles", are authentic letters of Paul. As for the latter three, the "Pastoral epistles", some scholars uphold the traditional view of these as the genuine writings of the Apostle Paul;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lock.2C_Meinertz_p.622_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lock.2C_Meinertz_p.622-101">[note 6]</a></sup> most, however, regard them as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudepigraphy" title="Pseudepigraphy">pseudepigrapha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>One might refer to the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Laodiceans" title="Epistle to the Laodiceans">Epistle to the Laodiceans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Third_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians" title="Third Epistle to the Corinthians">Third Epistle to the Corinthians</a> as examples of works identified as pseudonymous. Since the early centuries of the church, there has been debate concerning the authorship of the anonymous Epistle to the Hebrews, and contemporary scholars generally reject Pauline authorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrman_2004:411_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman_2004:411-29">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The epistles all share common themes, emphasis, vocabulary and style; they exhibit a uniformity of doctrine concerning the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Mosaic Law</a>, Jesus, faith, and various other issues. All of these letters easily fit into the chronology of Paul's journeys depicted in Acts of the Apostles.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_epistles">Other epistles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other epistles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The author of the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a> identifies himself in the opening verse as "James, a servant of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> and of the Lord <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> Christ". From the middle of the 3rd century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patristic" title="Patristic">patristic</a> authors cited the <i>Epistle</i> as written by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_the_Just" title="James the Just">James the Just</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[82]</a></sup> Ancient and modern scholars have always been divided on the issue of authorship. Many consider the epistle to be written in the late 1st or early 2nd centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>The author of the <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_Peter" title="First Epistle of Peter">First Epistle of Peter</a> identifies himself in the opening verse as "Peter, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" title="Apostle (Christian)">apostle</a> of Jesus Christ", and the view that the epistle was written by St. Peter is attested to by a number of <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>: <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a> (140–203), <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> (150–222), <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a> (155–215) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origen_of_Alexandria" title="Origen of Alexandria">Origen of Alexandria</a> (185–253). Unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Second_Epistle_of_Peter" title="The Second Epistle of Peter">The Second Epistle of Peter</a>, the authorship of which was debated in antiquity, there was little debate about Peter's authorship of this first epistle until the 18th century. Although 2 Peter internally purports to be a work of the apostle, many biblical scholars have concluded that Peter is not the author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[84]</a></sup> For an early date and (usually) for a defense of the Apostle Peter's authorship see Kruger,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[85]</a></sup> Zahn,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[86]</a></sup> Spitta,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[87]</a></sup> Bigg,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[88]</a></sup> and Green.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Epistle of Jude title is written as follows: "Jude, a servant of Jesus Christ and a brother of James" (<a href="/wiki/New_Revised_Standard_Version" title="New Revised Standard Version">NRSV</a>). The debate has continued over the author's identity as the apostle, the brother of Jesus, both, or neither.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Johannine_works">Johannine works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Johannine works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Johannine_works" title="Authorship of the Johannine works">Authorship of the Johannine works</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_John" title="First Epistle of John">First Epistle of John</a> is traditionally held to have been composed by <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a> (the author of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>) when the writer was in advanced age. The epistle's content, language and conceptual style indicate that it may have had the same author as the Gospel of John, 2 John and 3 John.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris_1John_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris_1John-112">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a> claimed that the author of 2nd and 3rd John was not <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a>, but an "elder John" which refers either to the apostle at an advanced age or a hypothetical second individual ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_the_Elder" title="John the Elder">John the Elder</a>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[92]</a></sup> Scholars today are divided on the issue.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revelation">Revelation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Revelation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The author of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> identifies himself several times as "John".<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Rev.+1%3A1%2C4%2C9%3B22%3A8&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Rev. 1:1, 4, 9; 22:8]</a></sup> and states that he was on <a href="/wiki/Patmos" title="Patmos">Patmos</a> when he received his first vision.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Rev.+1%3A9%3B4%3A1-2&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Rev. 1:9; 4:1-2]</a></sup> As a result, the author is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/John_of_Patmos" title="John of Patmos">John of Patmos</a>. The author has traditionally been identified with <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a> to whom the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistles_of_John" title="Epistles of John">epistles of John</a> were attributed. It was believed that he was exiled to the island of Patmos during the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Domitian" title="Domitian">Domitian</a>, and there wrote Revelation. <a href="/wiki/Justin_Martyr" title="Justin Martyr">Justin Martyr</a> (c. 100–165 AD) who was acquainted with <a href="/wiki/Polycarp" title="Polycarp">Polycarp</a>, who had been mentored by John, makes a possible allusion to this book, and credits John as the source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a> (c. 115–202) assumes it as a conceded point. According to the <i>Zondervan Pictorial Encyclopedia of the Bible</i>, modern scholars are divided between the apostolic view and several alternative hypotheses put forth in the last hundred years or so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[94]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ben_Witherington" title="Ben Witherington">Ben Witherington</a> points out that linguistic evidence makes it unlikely that the books were written by the same person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dates_of_composition">Dates of composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Dates of composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dd><i>See individual book articles for more detail.</i></dd>
</dl>
<p>The earliest works that became part of the New Testament are the letters of the Apostle Paul. The earliest of the books of the New Testament was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Thessalonians" title="First Thessalonians">First Thessalonians</a>, an epistle of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul</a>, written probably in 51, or possibly <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Galatians" title="Epistle to the Galatians">Galatians</a> in 49 according to one of two theories of its writing.</p>
<p>In the 1830s German scholars of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%C3%BCbingen_school" title="Tübingen school">Tübingen school</a> tried to date the books as late as the 3rd century, but the discovery of some <a href="/wiki/Biblical_manuscript" title="Biblical manuscript">New Testament manuscripts</a> and fragments from the 2nd and 3rd centuries, one of which dates as early as 125 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papyrus_52" title="Papyrus 52">Papyrus 52</a>), disproves a 3rd-century date of composition for any book now in the New Testament. Additionally, a <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_Clement" title="First Epistle of Clement">letter to the church at Corinth</a> in the name of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clement_I" title="Clement I">Clement of Rome</a> in 95 quotes from 10 of the 27 books of the New Testament, and a <a href="/wiki/Polycarp%27s_letter_to_the_Philippians" title="Polycarp's letter to the Philippians">letter to the church at Philippi in the name of Polycarp</a> in 120 quotes from 16 books. Therefore, some of the books of the New Testament must have been in circulation by the end of the first century.</p>
<p>Scholars hold a wide spectrum of views on exactly when the books of the new testament were written, with non-fundamentalist scholars tending to argue for later dates, and more conservative scholars arguing for an earlier one. Most contemporary scholars regard <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Mark</a> as a source used by Luke (see <a href="/wiki/Marcan_priority" title="Marcan priority">Marcan priority</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[96]</a></sup> If it is true that Mark was written around the destruction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jerusalem" title="Temple of Jerusalem">Temple of Jerusalem</a>, around 70,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TM1998_Mark_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-TM1998_Mark-118">[97]</a></sup> they theorize that Luke would not have been written before 70. Some who take this view believe that Luke's prediction of the destruction of the temple could not be a result of Jesus predicting the future but with the benefit of hindsight regarding specific details. They believe that the <a href="/wiki/Olivet_Discourse" title="Olivet Discourse">Olivet Discourse</a> in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Luke+21%3A5%E2%80%9330&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Luke 21:5–30</a></span> is specific enough (more specific than Mark's or Matthew's) that a date after 70 seems likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODCC_self_Matthew_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC_self_Matthew-119">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[note 7]</a></sup> These scholars have suggested dates for Luke from 75 to 100.</p>
<p>Support for a later date comes from a number of reasons. Differences of chronology, "style", and theology suggest that the author of Luke-Acts was not familiar with <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Christianity" title="Pauline Christianity">Paul's distinctive theology</a> but instead was writing a decade or more after his death, by which point significant harmonization between different traditions within <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a> had occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[100]</a></sup> Furthermore, Luke-Acts has views on <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Jesus' divine nature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">the end times</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soteriology" title="Soteriology">salvation</a> that are similar to those found in <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_epistles" title="Pastoral epistles">Pastoral epistles</a>, which are often seen as <a href="/wiki/Pseudonym" title="Pseudonym">pseudonymous</a> and of a later date than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Pauline_Epistles" title="Authorship of the Pauline Epistles">the undisputed</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pauline_Epistles" title="Pauline Epistles">Pauline Epistles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most conservative scholars however, argue that both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_evidence" title="Internal evidence">internal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/External_evidence" title="External evidence">external</a> evidence strongly points to dates prior to 70 AD for the <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_Gospels" title="Synoptic Gospels">Synoptic Gospels</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_Acts" title="Book of Acts">Acts</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pauline_Epistles" title="Pauline Epistles">Pauline Epistles</a>. They note that there is no mention of the deaths of <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Peter</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_the_Just" title="James the Just">James</a>, all of which happened between 60-65 AD, in any book of the New Testament. These were all extremely important figures in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early church</a>, writers would have mentioned their deaths if the New Testament had been written later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson-124">[102]</a></sup> Furthermore, the Gospels contain numerous attacks on the <a href="/wiki/Sadducees" title="Sadducees">Sadducees</a>, a sect of Judaism that was wiped out with the destruction of the temple. Why, they ask, would later writers devote so much narrative space to attacking a group that no longer existed?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-125">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Robinson_(bishop_of_Woolwich)" title="John Robinson (bishop of Woolwich)">John Robinson</a> also notes that each book of the New Testament had to be written prior to the destruction of The Temple. Robinson notes that most scholars interpret the <a href="/wiki/Olivet_Discourse" title="Olivet Discourse">Olivet Discourse</a> as a post 70 AD account of the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(AD_70)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (AD 70)">destruction of The Temple</a>, couched in language to make it appear to be a prophecy, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a> of Jesus to end the world. Robinson notes that the Second Coming did not occur after the destruction of The Temple, leading him to ask, why would a writer in the 80s or 90s forge a prophecy of an event that that is proven not to have occurred 20 years earlier?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson-124">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Language_of_the_New_Testament" title="Language of the New Testament">Language of the New Testament</a></div>
<p>The major languages spoken by both Jews and Greeks in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_and_historical_background_of_Jesus" title="Cultural and historical background of Jesus">time of Jesus</a> were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic_of_Jesus" title="Aramaic of Jesus">Aramaic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>, and also a colloquial dialect of <a href="/wiki/Mishnaic_Hebrew" title="Mishnaic Hebrew">Mishnaic Hebrew</a>. It is generally agreed by most scholars that the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">historical Jesus</a> primarily spoke <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic_of_Jesus" title="Aramaic of Jesus">Aramaic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[104]</a></sup> perhaps also some <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>. The majority view is that all of the books that would eventually form the New Testament were written in the Koine Greek language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">As Christianity spread</a>, these books were later translated into other languages, most notably, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Egyptian Coptic</a>. However, some of the <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[107]</a></sup> imply or claim that Matthew was originally written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew_Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Hebrew Gospel of Matthew">Hebrew</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic_Matthew" title="Aramaic Matthew">Aramaic</a>, and then soon after was written in Koine Greek. Nevertheless, the Gospel of Matthew known today was composed in Greek and is neither directly dependent upon nor a translation of a text in a <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon">Development of the New Testament canon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Development of the New Testament canon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">Development of the New Testament canon</a></div>
<p>The process of canonization of the New Testament was complex and lengthy. In the initial centuries of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christianity</a>, there were many books widely considered by the church to be inspired, but there was no single formally recognized New Testament canon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[109]</a></sup> The process was characterized by a compilation of books that <a href="/wiki/Sacred_tradition" title="Sacred tradition">apostolic tradition</a> considered authoritative in worship and teaching, relevant to the historical situations in which they lived, and consonant with the Old Testament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[110]</a></sup> Writings attributed to the apostles circulated among the <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">earliest Christian communities</a> and the Pauline epistles were circulating, perhaps in collected forms, by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_1st_century" title="Christianity in the 1st century">1st century AD</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the earliest attempts at solidifying a canon was made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcion" title="Marcion">Marcion</a>, <i>circa</i> 140 AD, who accepted only a modified version of Luke (the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Marcion" title="Gospel of Marcion">Gospel of Marcion</a>) and ten of Paul's letters, while rejecting the Old Testament entirely. His canon was increasingly rejected by other groups of Christians, notably the <a href="/wiki/Proto-orthodox_Christianity" title="Proto-orthodox Christianity">proto-orthodox Christians</a>, as was his theology, <a href="/wiki/Marcionism" title="Marcionism">Marcionism</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adolf_Harnack" title="Adolf Harnack">Adolf Harnack</a> in <i>Origin of the New Testament</i> (1914) observed that the church gradually formulated its New Testament canon in response to the challenge posed by Marcion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Justin_Martyr" title="Justin Martyr">Justin Martyr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> held the letters of Paul to be on par with the Hebrew Scriptures as being divinely inspired, yet others rejected him. Other books were held in high esteem but were gradually relegated to the status of <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">New Testament apocrypha</a>. Justin Martyr, in the mid <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_2nd_century" title="Christianity in the 2nd century">2nd century</a>, mentions "memoirs of the apostles" as being read on Sunday alongside the <a href="/wiki/Nevi%27im" title="Nevi'im">"writings of the prophets"</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-135">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Muratorian_fragment" title="Muratorian fragment">Muratorian fragment</a>, dated at between 170 and as late as the end of the 4th century (according to the <a href="/wiki/Anchor_Bible_Series#Anchor_Bible_Dictionary" title="Anchor Bible Series">Anchor Bible Dictionary</a>), may be the earliest known New Testament canon attributed to mainstream Christianity. It is similar, but not identical, to the modern New Testament canon.</p>
<p>The oldest clear endorsement of Mark, Matthew, Luke, and John being the only legitimate gospels was written <i>circa</i> 180 AD. A four gospel canon (the <i>Tetramorph</i>) was asserted by Irenaeus, who refers to it directly<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHIII8_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHIII8-137">[115]</a></sup> in his <a href="/wiki/Polemic" title="Polemic">polemic</a> <i>Against the Heresies</i>, "It is not possible that the gospels can be either more or fewer in number than they are. For, since there are four zones of the world in which we live, and four principal winds, while the church is scattered throughout all the world, and the "pillar and ground" of the church is the gospel and the spirit of life; it is fitting that she should have four pillars, breathing out immortality on every side, and vivifying men afresh."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHIII8_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHIII8-137">[115]</a></sup> The books considered to be authoritative by Irenaeus included the four gospels and many of the letters of Paul, although, based on the arguments Irenaeus made in support of only four authentic gospels, some interpreters deduce that the fourfold Gospel must have still been a novelty in Irenaeus's time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Origen_(3rd_century)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Origen_.283rd_century.29">Origen (3rd century)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Origen (3rd century)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the early 200s, <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> may have been using the same twenty-seven books as in the Catholic New Testament canon, though there were still disputes over the canonicity of the Letter to the Hebrews, Epistle of James, II Peter, II John and III John and the Book of Revelation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[117]</a></sup> known as the <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">Antilegomena</a>. Likewise, the <a href="/wiki/Muratorian_fragment" title="Muratorian fragment">Muratorian fragment</a> is evidence that, perhaps as early as 200, there existed a set of Christian writings somewhat similar to the twenty-seven book NT canon, which included four gospels and argued against objections to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[118]</a></sup> Thus, while there was a good measure of debate in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Church" title="Early Church">Early Church</a> over the New Testament canon, the major writings are claimed to have been accepted by almost all Christians by the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_3rd_century" title="Christianity in the 3rd century">3rd century</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Origen was largely responsible for the collection of usage information regarding the texts that became the New Testament. The information used to create the late-4th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Easter_Letter" title="Easter Letter">Easter Letter</a>, which declared accepted Christian writings, was probably based on the <i>Ecclesiastical History</i> [HE] of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius of Caesarea</a>, wherein he uses the information passed on to him by Origen to create both his list at HE 3:25 and Origen's list at HE 6:25. Eusebius got his information about what texts were then accepted and what were then <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">disputed</a>, by the <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">third-century churches throughout the known world</a>, a great deal of which Origen knew of firsthand from his extensive travels, from the library and writings of Origen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>In fact, Origen would have possibly included in his list of "inspired writings" other texts kept out by the likes of Eusebius—including the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Barnabas" title="Epistle of Barnabas">Epistle of Barnabas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shepherd_of_Hermas" title="Shepherd of Hermas">Shepherd of Hermas</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_Clement" title="1 Clement">1 Clement</a>. Notwithstanding these facts, "Origen is not the originator of the idea of biblical canon, but he certainly gives the philosophical and literary-interpretative underpinnings for the whole notion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Eusebius's_Ecclesiastical_History"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Eusebius.27s_Ecclesiastical_History">Eusebius's Ecclesiastical History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Eusebius's Ecclesiastical History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>, <i>circa</i> 300, gave a detailed list of New Testament writings in his <i>Ecclesiastical History</i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.ccel.org/fathers2/NPNF2-01/Npnf2-01-08.htm#P1497_696002" rel="nofollow">Book 3</a>, Chapter XXV:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"1... First then must be put the holy quaternion of the gospels; following them the Acts of the Apostles... the epistles of Paul... the epistle of John... the epistle of Peter... After them is to be placed, if it really seem proper, the Book of Revelation, concerning which we shall give the different opinions at the proper time. These then belong among the accepted writings."</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>"3 Among the disputed writings, which are nevertheless recognized by many, are extant the so-called epistle of James and that of Jude, also the second epistle of Peter, and those that are called the second and third of John, whether they belong to the evangelist or to another person of the same name. Among the rejected [Kirsopp Lake translation: "not genuine"] writings must be reckoned also the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Paul" title="Acts of Paul">Acts of Paul</a>, and the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shepherd_of_Hermas" title="Shepherd of Hermas">Shepherd</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse_of_Peter" title="Apocalypse of Peter">Apocalypse of Peter</a>, and in addition to these the extant <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Barnabas" title="Epistle of Barnabas">epistle of Barnabas</a>, and the so-called <a href="/wiki/Didache" title="Didache">Teachings of the Apostles</a>; and besides, as I said, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Apocalypse of John</a>, if it seem proper, which some, as I said, reject, but which others class with the accepted books. And among these some have placed also the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_the_Hebrews" title="Gospel of the Hebrews">Gospel according to the Hebrews</a>... And all these may be reckoned among the disputed books."</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>"6... such books as the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Peter" title="Gospel of Peter">Gospels of Peter</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Thomas" title="Gospel of Thomas">Thomas</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthias" title="Gospel of Matthias">Matthias</a>, or of any others besides them, and the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Andrew" title="Acts of Andrew">Acts of Andrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_John" title="Acts of John">John</a> and the other apostles... they clearly show themselves to be the fictions of heretics. Wherefore they are not to be placed even among the rejected writings, but are all of them to be cast aside as absurd and impious."</dd>
</dl>
<p>The Book of Revelation is counted as both accepted (Kirsopp Lake translation: "Recognized") and disputed, which has caused some confusion over what exactly Eusebius meant by doing so. From other writings of the church fathers, it was disputed with several canon lists rejecting its canonicity. EH 3.3.5 adds further detail on Paul: "Paul's fourteen epistles are well known and undisputed. It is not indeed right to overlook the fact that some have rejected the Epistle to the Hebrews, saying that it is disputed by the church of Rome, on the ground that it was not written by Paul." EH 4.29.6 mentions the <a href="/wiki/Diatessaron" title="Diatessaron">Diatessaron</a>: "But their original founder, Tatian, formed a certain combination and collection of the gospels, I know not how, to which he gave the title Diatessaron, and which is still in the hands of some. But they say that he ventured to paraphrase certain words of the apostle Paul, in order to improve their style."</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="4th_century_and_later">4th century and later</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 4th century and later">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his Easter letter of 367, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athanasius" title="Athanasius">Athanasius</a>, Bishop of Alexandria, gave a list of the books that would become the twenty-seven-book NT canon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LindbergCarter_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-LindbergCarter-144">[122]</a></sup> and he used the word "canonized" (<i>kanonizomena</i>) in regards to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[123]</a></sup> The first council that accepted the present canon of the New Testament may have been the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Hippo" title="Synod of Hippo">Synod of Hippo Regius</a> in North Africa (393 AD); the acts of this council, however, are lost. A brief summary of the acts was read at and accepted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage_(397)" title="Council of Carthage (397)">Council of Carthage (397)</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage_(419)" title="Council of Carthage (419)">Council of Carthage (419)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonaldD2_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDonaldD2-146">[124]</a></sup> These councils were under the authority of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">St. Augustine</a>, who regarded the canon as already closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferguson320_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson320-147">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Damasus_I" title="Pope Damasus I">Pope Damasus I</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Rome" title="Council of Rome">Council of Rome</a> in 382, if the <i><a href="/wiki/Decretum_Gelasianum" title="Decretum Gelasianum">Decretum Gelasianum</a></i> is correctly associated with it, issued a biblical canon identical to that mentioned above,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LindbergCarter_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-LindbergCarter-144">[122]</a></sup> or, if not, the list is at least a 6th-century compilation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce_1988_234_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce_1988_234-150">[128]</a></sup> Likewise, Damasus' commissioning of the Latin <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> edition of the Bible, c. 383, was instrumental in the fixation of the canon in the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce_1988_225_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce_1988_225-151">[129]</a></sup> In <i>c.</i> 405, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_I" title="Pope Innocent I">Pope Innocent I</a> sent a list of the sacred books to a Gallic bishop, <a href="/wiki/Exuperius" title="Exuperius">Exsuperius of Toulouse</a>. Christian scholars assert that, when these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishops" title="Bishops">bishops</a> and councils spoke on the matter, however, they were not defining something new but instead "were ratifying what had already become the mind of the Church."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferguson320_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson320-147">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metzger_1987_237.E2.80.93238_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metzger_1987_237.E2.80.93238-152">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce-153">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>The New Testament canon as it is now was first listed by <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_of_Alexandria" title="Athanasius of Alexandria">St. Athanasius, Bishop of Alexandria</a>, in 367, in a letter written to his churches in Egypt, <a class="external text" href="http://www.ccel.org/fathers2/NPNF2-04/Npnf2-04-93.htm" rel="nofollow">Festal Letter 39</a>. Also cited is the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Rome" title="Council of Rome">Council of Rome</a>, but not without controversy. That canon gained wider and wider recognition until it was accepted at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Carthage" title="Third Council of Carthage">Third Council of Carthage</a> in 397 and 419.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[note 8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even this council did not settle the matter, however. Certain books, referred to as <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">Antilegomena</a>, continued to be questioned, especially <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">James</a> and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Revelation</a>. Even as late as the 16th century, the Reformer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> questioned (but in the end did not reject) the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Jude" title="Epistle of Jude">Epistle of Jude</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Hebrews" title="Epistle to the Hebrews">Epistle to the Hebrews</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a>. To this day, German-language <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Luther Bibles</a> are printed with these four books at the end of the canon, rather than in their traditional order as in other editions of the Bible.</p>
<p>In light of this questioning of the canon of Scripture by Protestants in the 16th century, the (Roman Catholic) <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> reaffirmed the traditional western canon (i.e., the canon accepted at the 4th-century <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Rome" title="Council of Rome">Council of Rome</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage" title="Council of Carthage">Council of Carthage</a>), thus making the <a href="/wiki/Canon_of_Trent" title="Canon of Trent">Canon of Trent</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> Bible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Dogma" title="Roman Catholic Dogma">dogma</a> in the Catholic Church. Later, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XI" title="Pope Pius XI">Pope Pius XI</a> on 2 June 1927 decreed the <a href="/wiki/Comma_Johanneum" title="Comma Johanneum">Comma Johanneum</a> was open to dispute and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XII" title="Pope Pius XII">Pope Pius XII</a> on 3 September 1943 issued the encyclical <i><a href="/wiki/Divino_afflante_Spiritu" title="Divino afflante Spiritu">Divino afflante Spiritu</a></i>, which allowed translations based on other versions than just the Latin <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>, notably in English the <a href="/wiki/New_American_Bible" title="New American Bible">New American Bible</a>.</p>
<p>Thus, some claim that, from the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century#Defining_scripture" title="Christianity in the 4th century">4th century</a>, there existed unanimity in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Church" title="Western Church">West</a> concerning the New Testament canon (as it is today),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[132]</a></sup> and that, by the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_5th_century" title="Christianity in the 5th century">5th century</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Church" title="Eastern Church">Eastern Church</a>, with a few exceptions, had come to accept the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> and thus had come into harmony on the matter of the canon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P._R._Ackroyd_and_C._F._Evans.2C_eds._1970_305_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-P._R._Ackroyd_and_C._F._Evans.2C_eds._1970_305-156">[133]</a></sup> Nonetheless, full dogmatic articulations of the canon were not made until the <a href="/wiki/Canon_of_Trent" title="Canon of Trent">Canon of Trent</a> of 1546 for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thirty-Nine_Articles" title="Thirty-Nine Articles">Thirty-Nine Articles</a> of 1563 for the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession_of_Faith" title="Westminster Confession of Faith">Westminster Confession of Faith</a> of 1647 for <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jerusalem" title="Synod of Jerusalem">Synod of Jerusalem</a> of 1672 for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox" title="Greek Orthodox">Greek Orthodox</a>.</p>
<p>On the question of NT Canon formation generally, New Testament scholar Lee Martin McDonald has written that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[134]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Although a number of Christians have thought that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_council" title="Church council">church councils</a> determined what books were to be included in the biblical canons, a more accurate reflection of the matter is that the councils recognized or acknowledged those books that had already obtained prominence from usage among the various early Christian communities.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Christian scholars assert that when these bishops and councils spoke on the matter, they were not defining something new, but instead "were ratifying what had already become the mind of the Church".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metzger_1987_237.E2.80.93238_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Metzger_1987_237.E2.80.93238-152">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce-153">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some synods of the 4th century published lists of canonical books (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Hippo" title="Synod of Hippo">Hippo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage" title="Council of Carthage">Carthage</a>). The existing 27-book canon of the New Testament was reconfirmed (for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a>) in the 16th century with the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> (also called the Tridentine Council) of 1546,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[135]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thirty-Nine_Articles" title="Thirty-Nine Articles">Thirty-Nine Articles</a> of 1563 for the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession_of_Faith" title="Westminster Confession of Faith">Westminster Confession of Faith</a> of 1647 for <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synod_of_Jerusalem" title="Synod of Jerusalem">Synod of Jerusalem</a> of 1672 for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>. Although these councils did include statements about the canon, when it came to the New Testament they were only reaffirming the existing canon, including the <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">Antilegomena</a>.</p>
<p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Encyclopedia" title="Catholic Encyclopedia">Catholic Encyclopedia</a></i> article on the Canon of the New Testament: "The idea of a complete and clear-cut canon of the New Testament existing from the beginning, that is from Apostolic times, has no foundation in history. The Canon of the New Testament, like that of the Old, is the result of a development, of a process at once stimulated by disputes with doubters, both within and without the Church, and retarded by certain obscurities and natural hesitations, and which did not reach its final term until the dogmatic definition of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Tridentine Council</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 331, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I_and_Christianity" title="Constantine I and Christianity">Constantine I</a> commissioned Eusebius to deliver <a href="/wiki/Fifty_Bibles_of_Constantine" title="Fifty Bibles of Constantine">fifty Bibles</a> for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_of_Constantinople" title="Church of Constantinople">Church of Constantinople</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athanasius" title="Athanasius">Athanasius</a> (<i>Apol. Const. 4</i>) recorded Alexandrian scribes around 340 preparing Bibles for <a href="/wiki/Constans" title="Constans">Constans</a>. Little else is known, though there is plenty of speculation. For example, it is speculated that this may have provided motivation for canon lists, and that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus_Graecus_1209" title="Codex Vaticanus Graecus 1209">Codex Vaticanus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Codex_Sinaiticus" title="Codex Sinaiticus">Codex Sinaiticus</a> may be examples of these Bibles. Together with the <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Codex_Alexandrinus" title="Codex Alexandrinus">Codex Alexandrinus</a>, these are the earliest extant Christian Bibles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[137]</a></sup> There is no evidence among the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea#The_biblical_canon" title="First Council of Nicaea">canons of the First Council of Nicaea of any determination on the canon</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_manuscripts">Early manuscripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Early manuscripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1417" data-file-width="2362" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg/220px-Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg/330px-Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg/440px-Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Papyrus_Bodmer_VIII.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Papyrus Bodmer VIII, at the Biblioteca Apostolica Vaticana, showing 1 and 2 Peter.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Regius_(019).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="630" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG/220px-Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG/330px-Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG/440px-Codex_Regius_%28019%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Regius_(019).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Codex_Regius_(New_Testament)" title="Codex Regius (New Testament)">Codex Regius (L or <b>019</b>)</a>, an 8th-century Greek manuscript of the New Testament with strong affinities to <a href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus" title="Codex Vaticanus">Codex Vaticanus</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Testament_manuscripts" title="New Testament manuscripts">New Testament manuscripts</a></div>
<p>Like other literature from <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">antiquity</a>, the text of the New Testament was (prior to the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>) preserved and transmitted in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuscripts" title="Manuscripts">manuscripts</a>. Manuscripts containing at least a part of the New Testament number in the thousands. The earliest of these (like manuscripts containing other literature) are often very fragmentarily preserved. Some of these fragments have even been thought to date as early as the 2nd century (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_90" title="Papyrus 90">Papyrus 90</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_98" title="Papyrus 98">Papyrus 98</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_104" title="Papyrus 104">Papyrus 104</a>, and famously <a href="/wiki/Rylands_Library_Papyrus_P52" title="Rylands Library Papyrus P52">Rylands Library Papyrus P52</a>, though the early date of the latter has recently been called into question).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>For each subsequent century, more and more manuscripts survive that contain a portion or all of the books that were held to be part of the New Testament at that time (for example, the New Testament of the 4th-century <a href="/wiki/Codex_Sinaiticus" title="Codex Sinaiticus">Codex Sinaiticus</a>, once a complete Bible, contains the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Barnabas" title="Epistle of Barnabas">Epistle of Barnabas</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shepherd_of_Hermas" title="Shepherd of Hermas">Shepherd of Hermas</a>), though occasionally these manuscripts contain other works as well (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_72" title="Papyrus 72">Papyrus 72</a> and the Crosby-Schøyen Codex). The date when a manuscript was written, however, does not necessarily reflect the date of the form of text it contains. That is, later manuscripts can, and occasionally do, contain older forms of text or older readings.</p>
<p>Some of the more important manuscripts containing an early text of books of the New Testament are:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Chester_Beatty_Papyri" title="Chester Beatty Papyri">Chester Beatty Papyri</a> (Greek; the New Testament portions of which were copied in the 3rd century)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Bodmer_Papyri" title="Bodmer Papyri">Bodmer Papyri</a> (Greek and Coptic; the New Testament portions of which were copied in the 3rd and 4th centuries)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Codex_Bobiensis" title="Codex Bobiensis">Codex Bobiensis</a> (Latin; copied in the 4th century, but containing at least a 3rd-century form of text)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Uncial_0171" title="Uncial 0171">Uncial <b>0171</b></a> (Greek; copied in the late-third or early 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Syriac_Sinaiticus" title="Syriac Sinaiticus">Syriac Sinaiticus</a> (Syriac; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schoyen_Collection" title="Schoyen Collection">Schøyen Manuscript</a> 2560 (Coptic; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus" title="Codex Vaticanus">Codex Vaticanus</a> (Greek; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Codex_Sinaiticus" title="Codex Sinaiticus">Codex Sinaiticus</a> (Greek; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Codex_Vercellensis" title="Codex Vercellensis">Codex Vercellensis</a> (Latin; copied in the 4th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Curetonian_Gospels" title="Curetonian Gospels">Curetonian Gospels</a> (Syriac; copied in the 5th century)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Garima_Gospels" title="Garima Gospels">Garima Gospels</a> ( <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez language</a>, produced in the 5th through 6th century)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Textual_variation">Textual variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Textual variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Textual_variants_in_the_New_Testament" title="Textual variants in the New Testament">Textual variants in the New Testament</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism_of_the_New_Testament" title="Textual criticism of the New Testament">Textual criticism of the New Testament</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">Textual criticism</a> deals with the identification and removal of <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(linguistics)" title="Transcription (linguistics)">transcription</a> errors in the <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">texts</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuscripts" title="Manuscripts">manuscripts</a>. Ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scribes" title="Scribes">scribes</a> made errors or alterations (such as including non-authentic <a href="/wiki/Interpolation_(manuscripts)" title="Interpolation (manuscripts)">additions</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[139]</a></sup> The New Testament has been preserved in more than 5,800 <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> manuscripts, 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> manuscripts and 9,300 manuscripts in various other ancient languages including <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethiopic" title="Ethiopic">Ethiopic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>. Even if the <a href="/wiki/Greek_New_Testament" title="Greek New Testament">original Greek</a> versions were lost, the entire New Testament could still be assembled from the translations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998-163">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, there are so many quotes from the New Testament in early church documents and commentaries that the entire New Testament could also be assembled from these alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998-163">[140]</a></sup> Not all biblical manuscripts come from orthodox Christian writers. For example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnostic" title="Gnostic">Gnostic</a> writings of <a href="/wiki/Valentinus_(Gnostic)" title="Valentinus (Gnostic)">Valentinus</a> come from the 2nd century AD, and these Christians were regarded as heretics by the mainstream church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce.2C_F.F._1981_P_14_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce.2C_F.F._1981_P_14-164">[141]</a></sup> The sheer number of witnesses presents unique difficulties, but it also gives scholars a better idea of how close modern Bibles are to the original versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce.2C_F.F._1981_P_14_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce.2C_F.F._1981_P_14-164">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>On noting the large number of surviving ancient manuscripts, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bruce_Metzger" title="Bruce Metzger">Bruce Metzger</a> sums up the view on the issue by saying "The more often you have copies that agree with each other, especially if they emerge from different geographical areas, the more you can cross-check them to figure out what the original document was like. The only way they'd agree would be where they went back genealogically in a family tree that represents the descent of the manuscripts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998_163-2"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998-163">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>A similar type of textual criticism is applied to other ancient texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-631-23200-1_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-631-23200-1-165">[142]</a></sup> There are far fewer witnesses to classical texts than to the Bible, and unlike the New Testament where the earliest witnesses are often within a couple decades of the original, the earliest existing manuscripts of most classical texts were written about a millennium after their composition. For example, the earliest surviving copies of parts of the Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>' main work, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annals_of_Imperial_Rome" title="Annals of Imperial Rome">Annals of Imperial Rome</a> (written in 116 AD), come from a single manuscript written in 850 AD, although for other parts of his work, the earliest copies come from the 11th century, while other parts of his work have been lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998_163-3"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998-163">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest copies of <a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_War" title="The Jewish War">The Jewish War</a> by <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> (originally composed in the 1st century AD), in contrast, come from nine manuscripts written in the 10th, 11th and 12th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998_163-4"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998-163">[140]</a></sup> After the Bible, the next best preserved ancient work is Homer's <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a>, with 650 copies originating about 1,000 years after the original copy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998_163-5"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998-163">[140]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commentaries_on_the_Gallic_War" title="Commentaries on the Gallic War">Caesar's Commentaries on the Gallic War</a> (written in the 50s BC) survives in nine copies written in the 8th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce.2C_F.F._1981_P_11_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce.2C_F.F._1981_P_11-166">[143]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>' history of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peloponesian_War" title="Peloponesian War">Peloponesian War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>' history of the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Persian War</a> (both written in the 5th century BC) survives in about eight early copies, the oldest ones dating from the 10th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruce.2C_F.F._1981_P_11_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce.2C_F.F._1981_P_11-166">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biblical scholar <a href="/wiki/F._F._Bruce" title="F. F. Bruce">F. F. Bruce</a> has said "the evidence for our New Testament writings is ever so much greater than the evidence for many writings of classical authors, the authenticity of which no one dreams of questioning...It is a curious fact that historians have often been much readier to trust the New Testament records than have many theologians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpolations">Interpolations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Interpolations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In attempting to determine the original text of the New Testament books, some modern textual critics have identified sections as additions of material, centuries after the gospel was written. These are called <a href="/wiki/Interpolation_(manuscripts)" title="Interpolation (manuscripts)">interpolations</a>. In modern translations of the Bible, the results of textual criticism have led to certain verses, words and phrases being left out or marked as not original. According to <a href="/wiki/Bart_D._Ehrman" title="Bart D. Ehrman">Bart D. Ehrman</a>, "These scribal additions are often found in late medieval manuscripts of the New Testament, but not in the manuscripts of the earlier centuries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-168">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most modern Bibles have footnotes to indicate passages that have disputed source documents. Bible Commentaries also discuss these, sometimes in great detail. While many variations have been discovered between early copies of biblical texts, almost all have no importance, as they are variations in spelling, punctuation, or grammar. Also, many of these variants are so particular to the Greek language that they would not appear in translations into other languages. For example, order of words (i.e. "man bites dog" versus "dog bites man") often does not matter in Greek, so textual variants that flip the order of words often have no consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998_163-6"><a href="#cite_note-Strobel.2C_Lee_1998-163">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Outside of these unimportant variants, there are a couple variants of some importance, although even these are minor and can be left out of modern Bibles without affecting any matter of theology or interpretation.
