The two most commonly cited examples are the <a href="/wiki/Mark_16" title="Mark 16">last verses</a> of the <i>Gospel of Mark</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[148]</a></sup> and the story of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericope_Adulterae" title="Pericope Adulterae">adulterous woman</a> in the <i>Gospel of John</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-174">[151]</a></sup> Many scholars and critics also believe that the Comma Johanneum reference supporting the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> doctrine in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_John" title="1 John">1 John</a> to have been a later addition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metzger1994_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metzger1994-176">[153]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Norman_Geisler" title="Norman Geisler">Norman Geisler</a> and William Nix, "The New Testament, then, has not only survived in more manuscripts than any other book from antiquity, but it has survived in a purer form than any other great book—a form that is 99.5% pure"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[154]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="524" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg/220px-RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg/330px-RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg/440px-RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RossanoGospelsChristBeforePilate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Rossano_Gospels" title="Rossano Gospels">Rossano Gospels</a>, 6th century, a representative of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> text.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The often referred to Interpreter's Dictionary of the Bible, a book written to prove the validity of the New Testament, says: " A study of 150 Greek [manuscripts] of the Gospel of Luke has revealed more than 30,000 different readings... It is safe to say that there is not one sentence in the New Testament in which the [manuscript] is wholly uniform."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[155]</a></sup> Most of the variation took place within the first three Christian centuries.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Text-types">Text-types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Text-types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the 4th century, textual "families" or types of text become discernible among <a href="/wiki/Biblical_manuscript" title="Biblical manuscript">New Testament manuscripts</a>. A "text-type" is the name given to a family of texts with similar readings due to common ancestors and mutual correction. Many early manuscripts, however, contain individual readings from several different earlier forms of text. Modern texual critics have identified the following text-types among textual witnesses to the New Testament: The <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_text-type" title="Alexandrian text-type">Alexandrian text-type</a> is usually considered to generally preserve many early readings. It is represented, e.g., by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus_Graecus_1209" title="Codex Vaticanus Graecus 1209">Codex Vaticanus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Sinaiticus" title="Codex Sinaiticus">Codex Sinaiticus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bodmer_Papyri" title="Bodmer Papyri">Bodmer Papyri</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Western_text-type" title="Western text-type">Western text-type</a> is generally longer and can be paraphrastic, but can also preserve early readings. The <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles#Manuscripts" title="Acts of the Apostles">Western version of the Acts of the Apostles</a> is, notably, 8.5% longer than the Alexandrian form of the text. Examples of the Western text are found in <a href="/wiki/Codex_Bezae" title="Codex Bezae">Codex Bezae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Claromontanus" title="Codex Claromontanus">Codex Claromontanus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Washingtonianus" title="Codex Washingtonianus">Codex Washingtonianus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vetus_Latina" title="Vetus Latina">Old Latin</a> (i.e., Latin translations made prior to the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>), as well as in quotations by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcion" title="Marcion">Marcion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatian" title="Tatian">Tatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyprian" title="Cyprian">Cyprian</a>.</p>
<p>A text-type referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Caesarean_text-type" title="Caesarean text-type">Caesarean text-type</a>" and thought to have included witnesses such as <a href="/wiki/Codex_Koridethi" title="Codex Koridethi">Codex Koridethi</a> and minuscule 565, can today be described neither as "Caesarean" nor as a text-type as was previously thought. However, the Gospel of Mark in <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_45" title="Papyrus 45">Papyrus 45</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Washingtonianus" title="Codex Washingtonianus">Codex Washingtonianus</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Family_13" title="Family 13">Family 13</a> does indeed reflect a distinct type of text.</p>
<p>Increasing standardization of distinct (and once local) text-types eventually gave rise to the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_text-type" title="Byzantine text-type">Byzantine text-type</a>. Since most manuscripts of the New Testament do not derive from the first several centuries, that is, they were copied after the rise of the Byzantine text-type, this form of text is found the majority of extant manuscripts and is therefore often called the "Majority Text." As with all of the other (earlier) text-types, the Byzantine can also occasionally preserve early readings.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_criticism">Biblical criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Biblical criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">Biblical criticism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">Biblical criticism</a> is the scholarly "study and investigation of <a href="/wiki/Biblical_manuscript" title="Biblical manuscript">biblical writings</a> that seeks to make discerning judgments about these writings." Viewing biblical texts as having human rather than supernatural origins, it asks when and where a particular text originated; how, why, by whom, for whom, and in what circumstances it was produced; what influences were at work in its production; what sources were used in its composition; and what message it was intended to convey.</p>
<p>It will vary slightly depending on whether the focus is on the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, the letters of the New Testament, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canonical_Gospels" title="Canonical Gospels">Canonical Gospels</a>. It also plays an important role in the quest for the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">historical Jesus</a>. It also addresses the physical text, including the meaning of the words and the way in which they are used, its preservation, history, and integrity. Biblical criticism draws upon a wide range of scholarly disciplines including <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Oral_Tradition" title="Christian Oral Tradition">Oral Tradition studies</a>, history, and <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">religious studies</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishing_a_critical_text">Establishing a critical text</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Establishing a critical text">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Testament_manuscripts" title="New Testament manuscripts">New Testament manuscripts</a></div>
<p>The <a href="#Textual_variation">textual variation</a> among manuscript copies of books in the New Testament prompted attempts to discern the earliest form of text already in antiquity (e.g., by the 3rd-century Christian author <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>). The efforts began in earnest again during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, which saw a revival of the study of ancient Greek texts. During this period, modern <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">textual criticism</a> was born. In this context, <a href="/wiki/Christian_humanism" title="Christian humanism">Christian humanists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Valla" title="Lorenzo Valla">Lorenzo Valla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> promoted a return to the original Greek of the New Testament. This was the beginning of modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Testament_textual_criticism" title="New Testament textual criticism">New Testament textual criticism</a>, which over subsequent centuries would increasingly incorporate more and more manuscripts, in more languages (i.e., versions of the New Testament), as well as citations of the New Testament by ancient authors and the New Testament text in <a href="/wiki/Lectionary" title="Lectionary">lectionaries</a> in order to reconstruct the earliest recoverable form of the New Testament text and the history of changes to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[156]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_earlier_and_contemporaneous_literature">Relationship to earlier and contemporaneous literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Relationship to earlier and contemporaneous literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Non-canonical_books_referenced_in_the_Bible" title="Non-canonical books referenced in the Bible">Non-canonical books referenced in the Bible</a></div>
<p>Books that later formed the New Testament, like other Christian literature of the period, originated in a literary context that reveals relationships not only to other Christian writings, but also to <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">Graeco-Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judaism#Antiquity" title="Judaism">Jewish</a> works. Of singular importance is the extensive use of and interaction with the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Jewish Bible</a> and what would become the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Christian Old Testament</a>. Both implicit and explicit citations, as well as countless allusions, appear throughout the books of the New Testament, from the Gospels and Acts, to the Epistles, to the Apocalypse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_versions">Early versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Early versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first translations (usually called "versions") of the New Testament were made beginning already at the end of 2nd century. The earliest versions of the New Testament are the translations into the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[158]</a></sup> These three versions were made directly from the Greek, and are frequently cited in the apparatuses of modern critical editions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syriac">Syriac</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Syriac">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Syriac_versions_of_the_Bible" title="Syriac versions of the Bible">Syriac versions of the Bible</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1761" data-file-width="1445" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg/220px-RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg/330px-RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg/440px-RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RabulaGospelsFolio04vCanonTable.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Rabbula_Gospels" title="Rabbula Gospels">Rabbula Gospels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eusebian_Canons" title="Eusebian Canons">Eusebian Canons</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Syriac was spoken in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, and with dialect in <a href="/wiki/Syria_Palaestina" title="Syria Palaestina">Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palaestina_Secunda" title="Palaestina Secunda">Byzantine Palestine</a> where it was known as <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Palestinian_Aramaic" title="Jewish Palestinian Aramaic">Jewish Palestinian Aramaic</a>. Several Syriac translations were made and have come to us. Most of the Old Syriac, however, as well as the Philoxonian version have been lost.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tatian" title="Tatian">Tatian</a>, the Assyrian, created the <a href="/wiki/Diatessaron" title="Diatessaron">Diatessaron</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Gospel_harmony" title="Gospel harmony">gospel harmony</a> written in Syriac around 170 AD and the earliest form of the gospel not only in Syriac but probably also in Armenian.</p>
<p>In the 19th century, manuscript evidence was discovered for an "Old Syriac" version of the four distinct (i.e., not harmonized) gospels. These "separated" (Syriac: <i>da-Mepharreshe</i>) gospels, though old, have been shown to be later than the Diatessaron. The Old Syriac gospels are fragmentarily preserved in two manuscripts: the 5th-century <a href="/wiki/Curetonian_Gospels" title="Curetonian Gospels">Curetonian Syriac</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Sinaiticus" title="Syriac Sinaiticus">Sinaitic Syriac</a> from the 4th or 5th century.</p>
<p>No Old Syriac manuscripts of other portions of the New Testament survive, though Old Syriac readings, e.g. from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pauline_Epistles" title="Pauline Epistles">Pauline Epistles</a>, can be discerned in citations made by Eastern fathers and in later Syriac versions. The Old Syriac version is a representative of the <a href="/wiki/Western_text-type" title="Western text-type">Western text-type</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a> version was prepared in the beginning of the 5th century. It contains only 22 books (neither the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_epistles" title="General epistles">Minor Catholic Epistles</a> of 2 Peter, 2 and 3 John, and Jude, nor the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> were part of this translation).</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philoxenian" title="Philoxenian">Philoxenian</a> probably was produced in 508 for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philoxenus,_Bishop_of_Mabung" title="Philoxenus, Bishop of Mabung">Philoxenus, Bishop of Mabung</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin">Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Vetus_Latina" title="Vetus Latina">Vetus Latina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a></div>
<p>The Gospels were likely translated into Latin as early as the last quarter of the 2nd century in North Africa (<i>Afra</i>). Not much later, there were also European Latin translations (<i>Itala</i>). There are about 80 Old Latin mansucripts. The <a href="/wiki/Vetus_Latina" title="Vetus Latina">Vetus Latina</a> ("Old Latin") versions often contain readings with a Western type of text. (For the avoidance of confusion, these texts were written in <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a>, not the early version of the Latin language known as <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a>, pre 75 BC.)</p>
<p>The bewildering diversity of the Old Latin versions prompted <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> to prepare another translation into Latin — the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>. In many respects it was merely a revision of the Old Latin. There are currently around 8,000 manuscripts of the Vulgate.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coptic">Coptic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Coptic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coptic_versions_of_the_Bible" title="Coptic versions of the Bible">Coptic versions of the Bible</a></div>
<p>There are several dialects of the Coptic language: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bohairic" title="Bohairic">Bohairic</a> (northern dialect), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fayyumic" title="Fayyumic">Fayyumic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahidic" title="Sahidic">Sahidic</a> (southern dialect), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akhmimic" title="Akhmimic">Akhmimic</a>, and others. The first translation was made by at least the 3rd century into the Sahidic dialect (cop<sup>sa</sup>). This translation represents a mixed text, mostly <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_text-type" title="Alexandrian text-type">Alexandrian</a>, though also with <a href="/wiki/Western_text-type" title="Western text-type">Western</a> readings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>A Bohairic translation was made later, but existed already in the 4th century. Though the translation makes less use of Greek words than the Sahidic, it does employ some Greek grammar (e.g., in word-order and the use of particles such as the syntactic construction μεν — δε). For this reason, the Bohairic translation can be helpful in the reconstruction of the early Greek text of the New Testament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_ancient_translations">Other ancient translations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Other ancient translations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="844" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg/220px-Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg/330px-Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg/440px-Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Matthew%27s_Gospel_-_British_Library_Add._MS_59874_Ethiopian_Bible.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
BL Add. MS 59874 with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethiopic" title="Ethiopic">Ethiopic</a> <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The continued spread of Christianity, and the foundation of national churches, led to the translation of the Bible—often beginning with books from the New Testament—into a variety of other languages at a relatively early date: <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethiopic" title="Ethiopic">Ethiopic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soghdian" title="Soghdian">Soghdian</a>, and eventually <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_Slavonic_language" title="Church Slavonic language">Old Church Slavonic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nubian_languages" title="Nubian languages">Nubian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_translations">Modern translations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Modern translations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations" title="Bible translations">Bible translations</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Modern_Literal_Version" title="Modern Literal Version">Modern Literal Version</a> is the most recent literal translation of the books of the new testament, with the largest number of translation experts being involved in the final translation.</p>
<p>Historically, throughout the <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christian world</a> and in the context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_(Christian)" title="Mission (Christian)">Christian missionary activity</a>, the New Testament (or portions thereof) has been that part of the Christian Bible first translated into the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a>. The production of such translations grew out of the insertion of <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> <a href="/wiki/Gloss_(annotation)" title="Gloss (annotation)">glosses</a> in biblical texts, as well as out of the production of <a href="/wiki/Biblical_paraphrase" title="Biblical paraphrase">biblical paraphrases</a> and poetic renditions of stories from the life of Christ (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Heliand" title="Heliand">Heliand</a>).</p>
<p>The 16th century saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> and an explosion of translations of the New (and Old) Testament into the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a>. Notable are those of <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Martin Luther</a> (1522), <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Lef%C3%A8vre_d%27%C3%89taples" title="Jacques Lefèvre d'Étaples">Jacques Lefèvre d'Étaples</a> (1523), the <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich_Bible" title="Zürich Bible">Froschau Bible</a> (1525–1529, revised in 1574), <a href="/wiki/Tyndale_Bible" title="Tyndale Bible">William Tyndale</a> (1526, revised in 1534, 1535 and 1536), the <a href="/wiki/Brest_Bible" title="Brest Bible">Brest Bible</a> (1563), and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" title="Authorized King James Version">Authorized Version</a> (also called the "King James Version") (1611).</p>
<p>Most of these translations relied (though not always exclusively) upon one of the printed editions of the Greek New Testament edited by <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a>, a form of this Greek text emerged as the standard and is known as the <a href="/wiki/Textus_Receptus" title="Textus Receptus">Textus Receptus</a>. This text, based on the majority of manuscripts is also used in the majority of translations that were made in the years 100 to 400 AD.</p>
<p>Translations of the New Testament made since the appearance of critical editions of the Greek text (notably those of <a href="/wiki/Constantin_von_Tischendorf" title="Constantin von Tischendorf">Tischendorf</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_New_Testament_in_the_Original_Greek" title="The New Testament in the Original Greek">Westcott and Hort</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Von_Soden_catalogue" title="Von Soden catalogue">von Soden</a>) have largely used them as their <a href="/wiki/Source_text" title="Source text">base text</a>. Unlike the <a href="/wiki/Textus_Receptus" title="Textus Receptus">Textus Receptus</a>, these have a pronounced Alexandrian character. Standard critical editions are those of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Souter" title="Alexander Souter">Souter</a>, Vogels, Bover, Merk, and <a href="/wiki/Novum_Testamentum_Graece" title="Novum Testamentum Graece">Nestlé-Aland</a> (the text, though not the full critical apparatus of which is reproduced in the <a href="/wiki/United_Bible_Societies" title="United Bible Societies">United Bible Societies</a>' "Greek New Testament").</p>
<p>Notable translations of the New Testament based on these most recent critical editions include the <a href="/wiki/Revised_Standard_Version" title="Revised Standard Version">Revised Standard Version</a> (1946, revised in 1971), <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/La_Bible_de_J%C3%A9rusalem" title="fr:La Bible de Jérusalem">La Bible de Jérusalem</a> (1961, revised in 1973 and 2000), the <a href="/wiki/Einheits%C3%BCbersetzung" title="Einheitsübersetzung">Einheitsübersetzung</a> (1970, final edition 1979), the <a href="/wiki/New_American_Bible" title="New American Bible">New American Bible</a> (1970, revised in 1986), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traduction_Oecum%C3%A9nique_de_la_Bible" title="Traduction Oecuménique de la Bible">Traduction Oecuménique de la Bible</a> (1988, revised in 2004), and the <a href="/wiki/New_Revised_Standard_Version" title="New Revised Standard Version">New Revised Standard Version</a> (1989).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theological_interpretation_in_Christian_churches">Theological interpretation in Christian churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Theological interpretation in Christian churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biblical_authority" title="Biblical authority">Biblical authority</a></div>
<p>Though all Christian churches accept the New Testament as Scripture, they differ in their understanding of the nature, extent, and relevance of its authority. Views of the authoritativeness of the New Testament often depend on the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">inspiration</a></i>, which relates to the role of God in the formation of the New Testament. Generally, the greater the role of God in one's doctrine of inspiration, the more one accepts the <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrine</a> of <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">biblical inerrancy</a> and/or authoritativeness of the Bible. One possible source of confusion is that these terms are difficult to define, because many people use them interchangeably or with very different meanings. This article will use the terms in the following manner:</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Infallibility</i> relates to the absolute correctness of the Bible in matters of doctrine.</li>
<li><i>Inerrancy</i> relates to the absolute correctness of the Bible in factual assertions (including historical and scientific assertions).</li>
<li><i>Authoritativeness</i> relates to the correctness of the Bible in questions of practice in morality.</li>
</ul>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The self-witness of the Bible to its inspiration demands a commitment to its unity. The ultimate basis for unity is contained in the claim of divine inspiration in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2tim+3%3A16&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">2 Timothy 3:16</a></span> that "all Scripture is given by inspiration of God, and is profitable for doctrine, for reproof, for correction, for instruction in righteousness" (KJV). The term "inspiration" renders the Greek word <i>theopneustos</i>. This term only occurs here in the New Testament and literally means "God-breathed" (the chosen translation of the NIV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elwell_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elwell-186">[163]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>All of these concepts depend for their meaning on the supposition that the text of Bible has been properly interpreted, with consideration for the intention of the text, whether <a href="/wiki/Literal_and_figurative_language" title="Literal and figurative language">literal</a> history, <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegory</a> or poetry, etc. Especially the doctrine of inerrancy is variously understood according to the weight given by the interpreter to scientific investigations of the world.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unity_in_diversity">Unity in diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Unity in diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The notion of unity in diversity of Scripture claims that the Bible presents a noncontradictory and consistent message concerning God and redemptive history. The fact of diversity is observed in comparing the diversity of time, culture, authors' perspectives, literary genre, and the theological themes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elwell_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elwell-186">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies from many theologians considering the "unity in diversity" to be found in the New Testament (and the Bible as a whole) have been collected and summarized by New Testament theologian <a href="/wiki/Frank_Stagg_(theologian)" title="Frank Stagg (theologian)">Frank Stagg</a>. He describes them as some basic presuppositions, tenets, and concerns common among the New Testament writers, giving to the New Testament its "unity in diversity":</p>
<ol>
<li>The reality of God is never argued but is always assumed and affirmed</li>
<li>Jesus Christ is absolutely central: he is Lord and Savior, the foretold Prophet, the Messianic King, the Chosen, the way, the truth, and the light, the One through whom God the Father not only acted but through whom He came</li>
<li>The Holy Spirit came anew with Jesus Christ.</li>
<li>The Christian faith and life are a calling, rooted in divine election.</li>
<li>The plight of everyone as sinner means that each person is completely dependent upon the mercy and grace of God</li>
<li>Salvation is both God's gift and his demand through Jesus Christ, to be received by faith</li>
<li>The death and resurrection of Jesus are at the heart of the total event of which he was the center</li>
<li>God creates a people of his own, designated and described by varied terminology and analogies</li>
<li>History must be understood eschatologically, being brought along toward its ultimate goal when the kingdom of God, already present in Christ, is brought to its complete triumph</li>
<li>In Christ, all of God's work of creation, revelation, and redemption is brought to fulfillment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stagg_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stagg-187">[164]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<h3><span id="Roman_Catholicism,_Eastern_Orthodoxy,_and_Classical_Anglicanism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Catholicism.2C_Eastern_Orthodoxy.2C_and_Classical_Anglicanism">Roman Catholicism, Eastern Orthodoxy, and Classical Anglicanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Roman Catholicism, Eastern Orthodoxy, and Classical Anglicanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, there are two modes of Revelation: Scripture and <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Tradition" title="Apostolic Tradition">Tradition</a>. Both of them are interpreted by the teachings of the Church. The Roman Catholic view is expressed clearly in the <a href="/wiki/Catechism_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Catechism of the Catholic Church">Catechism of the Catholic Church</a> (1997):</p>
<blockquote>
<p>§ 82: As a result the Church, to whom the transmission and interpretation of Revelation is entrusted, does not derive her certainty about all revealed truths from the holy Scriptures alone. Both Scripture and Tradition must be accepted and honoured with equal sentiments of devotion and reverence.<br/>
§ 107: The inspired books teach the truth. Since therefore all that the inspired authors or sacred writers affirm should be regarded as affirmed by the Holy Spirit, we must acknowledge that the books of Scripture firmly, faithfully, and without error teach that truth which God, for the sake of our salvation, wished to see confided to the Sacred Scriptures.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In Catholic terminology the teaching office is called the <a href="/wiki/Magisterium" title="Magisterium">Magisterium</a>. The Catholic view should not be confused with the two-source theory. As the Catechism states in §§ 80 and 81, Revelation has "one common source ... two distinct modes of transmission."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>While many <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> writers distinguish between Scripture and Tradition, Bishop Kallistos Ware says that for the Orthodox there is only one source of the Christian faith, Holy Tradition, within which Scripture exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglicans</a> believe that "Holy Scripture containeth all things necessary to salvation", (Article VI), but also that the Catholic Creeds "ought thoroughly to be received and believed" (Article VIII), and that the Church "hath authority in Controversies of Faith" and is "a witness and keeper of Holy Writ" (Article XX).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[167]</a></sup> Classical Anglicanism, therefore, like Orthodoxy, holds that Holy Tradition is the only safe guardian against perversion and innovation in the interpretation of Scripture.</p>
<p>In the famous words of Thomas Ken, Bishop of Bath and Wells: "As for my religion, I dye in the holy catholic and apostolic faith professed by the whole Church before the disunion of East and West, more particularly in the communion of the Church of England, as it stands distinguished from all Papal and Puritan innovations, and as it adheres to the doctrine of the Cross."</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestantism">Protestantism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Protestantism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">sola scriptura</a>, Protestants believe that their traditions of faith, practice and interpretations carry forward what the scriptures teach, and so tradition is not a source of authority in itself. Their traditions derive authority from the Bible, and are therefore always open to reëvaluation. This openness to doctrinal revision has extended in <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">Liberal</a> Protestant traditions even to the reevaluation of the doctrine of Scripture upon which the Reformation was founded, and members of these traditions may even question whether the Bible is infallible in doctrine, inerrant in historical and other factual statements, and whether it has uniquely divine authority. However, the adjustments made by modern <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestants</a> to their doctrine of scripture vary widely.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="American_evangelical_and_fundamentalist_Protestantism">American evangelical and fundamentalist Protestantism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: American evangelical and fundamentalist Protestantism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Within the US, the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Statement_on_Biblical_Inerrancy" title="Chicago Statement on Biblical Inerrancy">Chicago Statement on Biblical Inerrancy</a> (1978) is a statement, articulating evangelical views on this issue. Paragraph four of its summary states: "Being wholly and verbally God-given, Scripture is without error or fault in all its teaching, no less in what it states about God's acts in creation, about the events of world history, and about its own literary origins under God, than in its witness to God's saving grace in individual lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="American_mainline_and_liberal_Protestantism">American mainline and liberal Protestantism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: American mainline and liberal Protestantism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mainline_(Protestant)" title="Mainline (Protestant)">Mainline</a> American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> denominations, including the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_USA" title="Presbyterian Church USA">Presbyterian Church USA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America" title="Episcopal Church in the United States of America">The Episcopal Church</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_in_America" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church in America">Evangelical Lutheran Church in America</a>, do not teach the doctrine of inerrancy as set forth in the Chicago Statement. All of these churches have more ancient doctrinal statements asserting the authority of scripture, but may interpret these statements in such a way as to allow for a very broad range of teaching—from evangelicalism to skepticism. It is not an impediment to ordination in these denominations to teach that the scriptures contain errors, or that the authors follow a more or less unenlightened ethics that, however appropriate it may have seemed in the authors' time, moderns would be very wrong to follow blindly.</p>
<p>For example, <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women" title="Ordination of women">ordination of women</a> is universally accepted in the mainline churches, <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> is condemned as a grievous social tragedy but not always a personal <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">sin</a> or a crime against an unborn person, and <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a> is sometimes recognized as a genetic propensity or morally neutral preference that should be neither encouraged nor condemned. In North America, the most contentious of these issues among these churches at the present time is how far the ordination of gay men and lesbians should be accepted.</p>
<p>Officials of the Presbyterian Church USA report: "We acknowledge the role of scriptural authority in the Presbyterian Church, but Presbyterians generally do not believe in biblical inerrancy. Presbyterians do not insist that every detail of chronology or sequence or prescientific description in scripture be true in literal form. Our confessions do teach biblical infallibility. Infallibility affirms the entire truthfulness of scripture without depending on every exact detail."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Those who hold a more liberal view of the Bible as a human witness to the glory of God, the work of fallible humans who wrote from a limited experience unusual only for the insight they have gained through their inspired struggle to know God in the midst of a troubled world. Therefore, they tend not to accept such doctrines as inerrancy. These churches also tend to retain the social activism of their evangelical forebears of the 19th century, placing particular emphasis on those teachings of scripture that teach compassion for the poor and concern for <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>.</p>
<p>The message of personal <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> is, generally speaking, of the good that comes to oneself and the world through following the New Testament's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethic_of_reciprocity" title="Ethic of reciprocity">Golden Rule</a> admonition to love others without hypocrisy or prejudice. Toward these ends, the "spirit" of the New Testament, more than the letter, is infallible and authoritative.</p>
<p>There are some movements that believe the Bible contains the teachings of Jesus but who reject the churches that were formed following its publication. These people believe all individuals can communicate directly with God and therefore do not need guidance or doctrines from a church. These people are known as <a href="/wiki/Christian_anarchism" title="Christian anarchism">Christian anarchists</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Messianic_Judaism">Messianic Judaism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Messianic Judaism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Messianic_Judaism" title="Messianic Judaism">Messianic Judaism</a> generally holds the same view of New Testament authority as evangelical Protestants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beit_Simcha_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beit_Simcha-193">[170]</a></sup> According to the view of some Messianic Jewish congregations, Jesus did not annul the Torah, but that its interpretation is revised and ultimately explained through the Apostolic Scriptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KehilatTNuvahSOF1_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-KehilatTNuvahSOF1-194">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Jehovah's_Witnesses"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jehovah.27s_Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Jehovah's Witnesses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Christian Congregation of Jehovah's Witnesses</a> accepts the New Testament as divinely inspired Scripture, and as infallible in every detail, with equal authority as the Hebrew Scriptures. They view it as the written revelation and good news of the <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Atonement_in_Christianity" title="Atonement in Christianity">ransom sacrifice of Jesus</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God" title="Kingdom of God">Kingdom of God</a>, explaining and expounding the Hebrew Bible, not replacing but vitally supplementing it. They also view the New Testament as the primary instruction guide for Christian living, and <a href="/wiki/Church_discipline" title="Church discipline">church discipline</a>. They generally call the New Testament the "Christian Greek Scriptures", and see only the "covenants" as "old" or "new", but not any part of the actual Scriptures themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Pentecostals">United Pentecostals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: United Pentecostals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Oneness_Pentecostalism" title="Oneness Pentecostalism">Oneness Pentecostalism</a> subscribes to the common Protestant doctrine of <i><a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">sola scriptura</a></i>. They view the Bible as the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">inspired</a> Word of God, and as absolutely <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">inerrant</a> in its contents (though not necessarily in every translation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[174]</a></sup> They regard the New Testament as perfect and inerrant in every way, revealing the Lord Jesus Christ in the Flesh, and his Atonement, and which also explains and illuminates the Old Testament perfectly, and is part of the Bible canon, not because church councils or decrees claimed it so, but by witness of the Holy Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seventh-Day_Adventists">Seventh-Day Adventists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Seventh-Day Adventists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church">Seventh-day Adventist Church</a> holds the New Testament as the inspired Word of God, with God influencing the "thoughts" of the Apostles in the writing, not necessarily every word though. The first fundamental belief of the Seventh-Day Adventist church stated that "The Holy Scriptures are the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_infallibility" title="Biblical infallibility">infallible</a> revelation of [God's] will." <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_theology" title="Seventh-day Adventist theology">Adventist theologians</a> generally reject the "verbal inspiration" position on Scripture held by many conservative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Christians" title="Evangelical Christians">evangelical Christians</a>. They believe instead that God inspired the thoughts of the biblical authors and apostles, and that the writers then expressed these thoughts in their own words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[177]</a></sup> This view is popularly known as "thought inspiration", and most Adventist members hold to that view. According to Ed Christian, former <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_the_Adventist_Theological_Society" title="Journal of the Adventist Theological Society">JATS</a></i> editor, "few if any <a href="/wiki/Adventist_Theological_Society" title="Adventist Theological Society">ATS</a> members believe in verbal inerrancy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding the teachings of the New Testament compared to the Old, and the application in the New Covenant, Adventists have traditionally taught that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethical_Decalogue" title="Ethical Decalogue">Decalogue</a> is part of the moral law of God, which was not abrogated by the ministry and death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>. Therefore, the fourth commandment concerning the Sabbath is as applicable to Christian believers as the other nine. Adventists have often taught a distinction between "moral law" and "ceremonial law". According to Adventist beliefs, the moral law continues into the "New Testament era", but the ceremonial law was done away with by Jesus.</p>
<p>How the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosaic_law" title="Mosaic law">Mosaic law</a> should be applied came up at Adventist conferences in the past, and Adventist theologians such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A._T._Jones" title="A. T. Jones">A. T. Jones</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._J._Waggoner" title="E. J. Waggoner">E. J. Waggoner</a> looked at the problem addressed by Paul in <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Galatians" title="Epistle to the Galatians">Galatians</a> as not the ceremonial law, but rather the wrong use of the law (<a href="/wiki/Legalism_(theology)" title="Legalism (theology)">legalism</a>). They were opposed by <a href="/wiki/Uriah_Smith" title="Uriah Smith">Uriah Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Ide_Butler" title="George Ide Butler">George Butler</a> at the 1888 Conference. Smith in particular thought the Galatians issue had been settled by Ellen White already, yet in 1890 she claimed justification by faith is "the <a href="/wiki/Three_Angels%27_Messages" title="Three Angels' Messages">third angel's message</a> in verity."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Ellen White interpreted <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblica.com/en-us/bible/online-bible/?osis=niv:Colossians.2:14–2:14" rel="nofollow">Colossians 2:14</a> as saying that the ceremonial law was nailed to the cross.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[179]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latter-day_Saints">Latter-day Saints</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Latter-day Saints">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Members of <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> (LDS Church) believe that the New Testament, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_biblical_canons" title="Christian biblical canons">Christian biblical canon</a>, is accurate "as far as it is translated correctly".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[180]</a></sup> They believe the Bible as originally revealed is the word of God, but that the processes of transcription and translation have introduced errors into the texts as currently available, and therefore they cannot be regarded as completely inerrant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Givens2015_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Givens2015-204">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LDSInerrancy_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-LDSInerrancy-205">[182]</a></sup> In addition to the Old and New Testaments, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon:_Another_Testament_of_Jesus_Christ" title="Book of Mormon: Another Testament of Jesus Christ">Book of Mormon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_and_Covenants" title="Doctrine and Covenants">Doctrine and Covenants</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_of_Great_Price_(Mormonism)" title="Pearl of Great Price (Mormonism)">Pearl of Great Price</a> are considered part of their scriptural canon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PaulsenMusser2007_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-PaulsenMusser2007-207">[184]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_liturgy">In the liturgy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: In the liturgy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Harcleianus.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="2200" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Codex_Harcleianus.PNG/220px-Codex_Harcleianus.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Codex_Harcleianus.PNG/330px-Codex_Harcleianus.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Codex_Harcleianus.PNG/440px-Codex_Harcleianus.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Harcleianus.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Byzantine lectionary, <i><a href="/wiki/Lectionary_150" title="Lectionary 150">Codex Harleianus</a></i> (<i>l</i><sup>150</sup>), 995 AD, text of John 1:18.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite the wide variety among <a href="/wiki/Christian_liturgy" title="Christian liturgy">Christian liturgies</a>, texts from the New Testament play a role in almost all forms of <a href="/wiki/Christian_worship" title="Christian worship">Christian worship</a>. In addition to some language derived from the New Testament in the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a> itself (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Trisagion" title="Trisagion">Trisagion</a> may be based on Apocalypse 4:8, and the beginning of the "Hymn of Praise" draws upon Luke 2:14), the reading of extended passages from the New Testament is a practice common to almost all <a href="/wiki/Christian_worship" title="Christian worship">Christian worship</a>, liturgical or not.</p>
<p>These <a href="/wiki/Lection" title="Lection">readings</a> are most often part of an established <a href="/wiki/Lectionary" title="Lectionary">lectionary</a> (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Lection" title="Lection">selected texts</a> to be read at church services on specific days), and (together with an Old Testament reading and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psalms_(Christian)" title="Psalms (Christian)">Psalm</a>) include a non-gospel reading from the New Testament and culminate with a <a href="/wiki/Gospel_(liturgy)" title="Gospel (liturgy)">Gospel reading</a>. No readings from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a>, however, are included in the standard <a href="/wiki/Lectionary" title="Lectionary">lectionary</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_church" title="Orthodox church">Eastern Orthodox churches</a>.</p>
<p>Central to the <a href="/wiki/Christian_liturgy" title="Christian liturgy">Christian liturgy</a> is the celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> or "Holy Communion". The <a href="/wiki/Words_of_Institution" title="Words of Institution">Words of Institution</a> that begin this rite are drawn directly from 1 Corinthians 11:23–26. In addition, the communal recitation of the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord's Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a> (in the form found in the Gospel of Matthew 6:9–13) is also a standard feature of <a href="/wiki/Christian_worship" title="Christian worship">Christian worship</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_arts">In the arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: In the arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus_in_art" title="Nativity of Jesus in art">Nativity of Jesus in art</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passion_play" title="Passion play">Passion play</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GaudenzioFerrari_StorieCristo_Varallo2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="524" data-file-width="972" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/GaudenzioFerrari_StorieCristo_Varallo2.jpg/220px-GaudenzioFerrari_StorieCristo_Varallo2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/GaudenzioFerrari_StorieCristo_Varallo2.jpg/330px-GaudenzioFerrari_StorieCristo_Varallo2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/GaudenzioFerrari_StorieCristo_Varallo2.jpg/440px-GaudenzioFerrari_StorieCristo_Varallo2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GaudenzioFerrari_StorieCristo_Varallo2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gaudenzio_Ferrari" title="Gaudenzio Ferrari">Gaudenzio Ferrari</a>'s <i>Stories of the Life and Passion of Christ</i>, fresco, 1513, Church of Santa Maria delle Grazie, Varallo Sesia, Italy. Depicting the life of Jesus</div>
</div>
</div>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tr>
<td style="width:1px"></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
<div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:G.F.H%C3%A4ndel-Halleluja_hoerprobe.ogg" title="File:G.F.Händel-Halleluja hoerprobe.ogg">"Hallelujah" chorus</a></div>
<div>
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="173.90789115646" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="G.F.Händel-Halleluja_hoerprobe.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="146941" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (147 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/G.F.H%C3%A4ndel-Halleluja_hoerprobe.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/></audio></div>
</div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">The text of the famous "Hallelujah" chorus in G. F. Händel's <i>Messiah</i> is drawn from three passages in the Book of Revelation: 19:6, 11:5, and 19:16 (audio clip from the German translation of the <i>Messiah</i>).</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
<hr/>
<span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Most of the influence of the New Testament upon <a href="/wiki/The_arts" title="The arts">the arts</a> has come from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gospels" title="Gospels">Gospels</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Literary expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">Nativity of Jesus</a> found in the Gospels of Matthew and Luke began already in the 2nd century, and the portrayal of the Nativity has continued in various art forms to this day. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christian_art" title="Early Christian art">earliest Christian art</a> would often depict scenes from the New Testament such as the <a href="/wiki/Raising_of_Lazarus" title="Raising of Lazarus">raising of Lazarus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">baptism of Jesus</a> or the motif of the <a href="/wiki/Good_Shepherd" title="Good Shepherd">Good Shepherd</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biblical_paraphrase" title="Biblical paraphrase">Biblical paraphrases</a> and poetic renditions of stories from the life of Christ (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Heliand" title="Heliand">Heliand</a>) became popular in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, as did the portrayal of the <a href="/wiki/Arrest_of_Jesus" title="Arrest of Jesus">arrest</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trial_of_Jesus" title="Trial of Jesus">trial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">execution</a> of Jesus in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passion_play" title="Passion play">Passion plays</a>. Indeed, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passion_(Christianity)" title="Passion (Christianity)">Passion</a> became a central theme in Christian <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_in_the_arts" title="Crucifixion in the arts">art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Passion_(music)" title="Passion (music)">music</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Category:Portrayals_of_Jesus_in_film" title="Category:Portrayals of Jesus in film">ministry</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passion_(Christianity)#Film" title="Passion (Christianity)">Passion</a> of Jesus, as portrayed in one or more of the <a href="/wiki/Category:Films_based_on_the_Gospels" title="Category:Films based on the Gospels">New Testament Gospels</a>, has also been a theme in film, almost since the inception of the medium (e.g., "La Passion", France, 1903).</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See also Tertullian, <a class="external text" href="http://earlychristianwritings.com/text/tertullian124.html" rel="nofollow"><i>Against Marcion, Book IV</i></a>, chapters I, II, XIV. However, his meaning in chapter XX is less clear, and in chapters IX and XL he uses the term to mean "new covenant".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Barber_Lightfoot" title="Joseph Barber Lightfoot">Joseph Barber Lightfoot</a> in his <i>Commentary on the Epistle to the Galatians</i> writes: "At this point [<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Gal+6%3A11&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Gal 6:11</a></span>] the apostle takes the pen from his <a href="/wiki/Amanuensis" title="Amanuensis">amanuensis</a>, and the concluding paragraph is written with his own hand. From the time when letters began to be forged in his name (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2+Thess+2%3A2&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">2 Thess. 3:17</a></span>; <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2+Thess+3%3A17&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">3:17</a></span>) it seems to have been his practice to close with a few words in his own handwriting, as a precaution against such forgeries.... In the present case he writes a whole paragraph, summing up the main lessons of the epistle in terse, eager, disjointed sentences. He writes it, too, in large, bold characters (Gr. <i>pelikois grammasin</i>), that his handwriting may reflect the energy and determination of his soul."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Gospels are in this order in many Old Latin manuscripts, as well as in the Greek manuscripts <a href="/wiki/Codex_Bezae" title="Codex Bezae">Codex Bezae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Codex_Washingtonianus" title="Codex Washingtonianus">Codex Washingtonianus</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See also the article on the <a href="/wiki/Antilegomena" title="Antilegomena">Antilegomena</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Hebrews was almost certainly not written by Paul, it has been a part of the Pauline corpus "from the beginning of extant MS production".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hebot01_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-hebot01-98">[79]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Lock.2C_Meinertz_p.622-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Lock.2C_Meinertz_p.622_101-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lock.2C_Meinertz_p.622_101-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Guthrie lists: ohlenberg, Lock, Meinertz, Thornell, Schlatter, Spicq, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jeremais&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jeremais (page does not exist)">Jeremias</a>, Simpson, Kelly, and Fee"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[80]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">S. Brown agrees that the references to the Jerusalem temple's destruction are seen as evidence of a post-70 date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[99]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">Book of Revelation</a> wasn't added till the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage_(419)" title="Council of Carthage (419)">Council of Carthage (419)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonaldD2_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-McDonaldD2-146">[124]</a></sup></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Eusebius. "Chapter 25". <a class="external text" href="http://www.newadvent.org/fathers/250106.htm" rel="nofollow"><i>Church History, Book VI</i></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANew+Testament&amp;rft.atitle=Chapter+25&amp;rft.au=Eusebius&amp;rft.btitle=Church+History%2C+Book+VI&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.newadvent.org%2Ffathers%2F250106.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Ehrman_2004:411-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Ehrman_2004:411_29-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ehrman_2004:411_29-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Ehrman, Bart D. (1997). <i>The New Testament: A Historical Introduction to the Early Christian Writings</i>. Oxford University Press. p. 323. "Scholars in the ancient world went about detecting forgeries in much the same way that modern scholars do. They looked to see whether the ideas and writing style of a piece conformed with those used by the author in other writings, and they examined the text for any blatant anachronisms, that is, statements about things that could not have existed at the time the alleged author was writing (like the letter reputedly from an early seventeenth-century American colonist that mentions "the United States")- Arguments of this kind were used by some Christian scholars of the third century to show that Hebrews was not written by Paul or the Book of Revelation by John the son of Zebedee. Modern scholars, as we will see, concur with these judgments. To be sure, neither of these books can be considered a forgery. Hebrews does not claim to be written by Paul (it is anonymous), and the John who wrote Revelation does not claim to be the son of Zebedee (it is therefore homonymous). Are there other books in the New Testament, though, that can be considered forgeries?"</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-lost-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-lost_61-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Bart_D._Ehrman" title="Bart D. Ehrman">Ehrman, Bart D.</a> (2005). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=URdACxKubDIC&amp;pg=PA235" rel="nofollow"><i>Lost Christianities: The Battles for Scripture and the Faiths We Never Knew</i></a>. Oxford University Press. p. 235. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-518249-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-518249-1">978-0-19-518249-1</a>. <q>The four Gospels that eventually made it into the New Testament, for example, are all anonymous, written in the third person <i>about</i> Jesus and his companions. None of them contains a first-person narrative ('One day, when Jesus and I went into Capernaum...'), or claims to be written by an eyewitness or companion of an eyewitness. ... Most scholars today have abandoned these identifications, and recognize that the books were written by otherwise unknown but relatively well-educated Greek-speaking (and writing) Christians during the second half of the first century.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANew+Testament&amp;rft.aufirst=Bart+D.&amp;rft.aulast=Ehrman&amp;rft.btitle=Lost+Christianities%3A+The+Battles+for+Scripture+and+the+Faiths+We+Never+Knew&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-518249-1&amp;rft.pages=235&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DURdACxKubDIC%26pg%3DPA235&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Carolina2004-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Carolina2004_62-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Bart_D._Ehrman" title="Bart D. Ehrman">Ehrman, Bart D.</a> (1 November 2004). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=vbLK6kn5T-EC&amp;pg=PA110" rel="nofollow"><i>Truth and Fiction in The Da Vinci Code: A Historian Reveals What We Really Know about Jesus, Mary Magdalene, and Constantine</i></a>. Oxford University Press, USA. p. 110. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-534616-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-534616-9">978-0-19-534616-9</a>. <q>In fact, contrary to what you might think, these Gospels don't even claim to be written by eyewitnesses.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANew+Testament&amp;rft.aufirst=Bart+D.&amp;rft.aulast=Ehrman&amp;rft.btitle=Truth+and+Fiction+in+The+Da+Vinci+Code%3A+A+Historian+Reveals+What+We+Really+Know+about+Jesus%2C+Mary+Magdalene%2C+and+Constantine&amp;rft.date=2004-11-01&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-534616-9&amp;rft.pages=110&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press%2C+USA&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DvbLK6kn5T-EC%26pg%3DPA110&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-iuda-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-iuda_63-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-iuda_63-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Bart_D._Ehrman" title="Bart D. Ehrman">Ehrman, Bart D.</a> (1 September 2006). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=oBuJMhJlTYwC&amp;pg=PA143" rel="nofollow"><i>The Lost Gospel of Judas Iscariot: A New Look at Betrayer and Betrayed</i></a>. Oxford University Press, USA. p. 143. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-971104-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-971104-8">978-0-19-971104-8</a>. <q>The Gospels of the New Testament are therefore our earliest accounts. These do not claim to be written by eyewitnesses to the life of Jesus, and historians have long recognized that they were produced by second- or third-generation Christians living in different countries than Jesus (and Judas) did, speaking a different language (Greek instead of Aramaic), experiencing different situations, and addressing different audiences.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANew+Testament&amp;rft.aufirst=Bart+D.&amp;rft.aulast=Ehrman&amp;rft.btitle=The+Lost+Gospel+of+Judas+Iscariot%3A+A+New+Look+at+Betrayer+and+Betrayed&amp;rft.date=2006-09-01&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-971104-8&amp;rft.pages=143&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press%2C+USA&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DoBuJMhJlTYwC%26pg%3DPA143&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Three forms are postulated, from <i>The Canon Debate</i>, chapter 18, page 300, note 21, attributed to Harry Y. Gamble: "(1) Marcion's collection that begins with Galatians and ends with Philemon; (2) Papyrus 46, dated about 200, that follows the order that became established except for reversing Ephesians and Galatians; and (3) the letters to seven churches, treating those to the same church as one letter and basing the order on length, so that Corinthians is first and Colossians (perhaps including Philemon) is last."</span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Testament&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Raymond_E._Brown" title="Raymond E. Brown">Brown, Raymond E.</a> (1997). <i>An Introduction to the New Testament</i>. Anchor Bible Reference Library; New York: Doubleday.</li>
